The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Acceptance of Diyah
Fragmented OCR showing dispute structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on diyah acceptance by identifying the schools' positions, the structure of objections and responses, and the role of invalid reasons (wujuh fasida) in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A single dispute reveals how schools differ in defining cause, intention, and validity.

This lesson reconstructs a heavily corrupted OCR passage from a Hanafi usul work. Despite the damage, the headings outline a dispute with the Shafi'i school regarding the acceptance of diyah (blood money) in marital dissolution scenarios. We will use the typical usul dispute structure—positions, objections, responses, and analysis of invalid reasons—to understand the legal reasoning at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
From the heading 'احناف کے ہاں دیانیہ بھی اسکی نیست معتبر نہیں' (According to Hanafis, diyya of his wife is not considered valid), the dispute concerns the validity of accepting diyah in a specific marital context, likely a divorce situation like 'taliq nafsak'.
The exact context is unclear due to OCR, but the mention of 'تخریج نیت' (extraction of intention) suggests a link to conditional divorce.
2
Extract the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
The Hanafi position stated: diyya is not valid (معتبر نہیں). The Shafi'i position is implied by the phrase 'شوافع کی دلیل' (evidence of Shafi'is), indicating they argue for its validity.
No dalil text is recoverable, but the structure shows a typical statement-evidence-response pattern.
3
Analyze the structure of objections and responses
The text includes 'اعتراض' (objection) and 'جواب' (response), indicating a dialectical presentation. This is a common usul format where a school presents an objection, then refutes it.
In Hanafi usul, such exchanges help clarify the school's tarjih (preponderance) over opposing views.
4
Note the concept of 'wajh fasid' as a tool in the dispute
The passage lists multiple 'وجہ فاسد' (invalid reasons). These are causes that appear valid but are legally corrupt, thus nullifying the effect. Understanding fasid reasons is key to distinguishing valid from invalid legal reasoning.
The enumeration of eight invalid reasons (sixth, seventh, etc.) suggests the text systematically debunks Shafi'i arguments by showing their underlying reasons are fasid.

Commentary
This passage is an OCR-damaged collection of headings and fragmented text from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work. The original discussion appears to involve a dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the validity of diyya (blood money) in certain contexts, possibly related to marriage dissolution (taliq nafsak). The text lists 'invalid reasons' (wujuh fasida) and presents objections and responses. Later sections touch on mutlaq and muqayyad, a chapter on stolen goods, and concessions. Due to severe OCR corruption, no continuous legal argument can be reconstructed. The Hanafi position is implied in the first heading but without full context.

Key Terms
دِیَہ (diyah): Blood money or financial compensation paid to the victim's family in cases of homicide or bodily harm.
وجہ فاسد (wajh fasid): An invalid reason or corrupt cause that nullifies a legal act.
تفریح (tafri'a): A branch or sub-discussion in a fiqh/usul text, often exploring a specific aspect of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To describe a valid reason that both schools accept.
B) To identify a flaw in the opponent's reasoning that invalidates their conclusion.
C) To list the different types of diyah payments.
D) To explain the conditions for valid divorce.

Key Takeaway
Even a fragmented usul text can teach the essential structure of a madhhab dispute: position, evidence, objection, response, and the critical analysis of invalid reasons (wujuh fasida).

Why Qiyas is Fourth: Epistemic Rank and Refutation of Deniers
Defending Qiyas as the fourth principle and clarifying its epistemic rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis rank Qiyas as the fourth source, distinguish its epistemic status (zanni vs qat'i) from Kitab, Sunnah, and Ijma, and identify the argument used to refute deniers of Qiyas.

Core Idea
Qiyas is generally zanni, while the first three are generally qat'i — but qiyas based on a qat'i text is itself qat'i.

This passage tackles a key objection: if Qiyas is an independent source, why isn't it simply listed as the fourth? The author clarifies that while Qiyas is a source, its epistemic weight is generally speculative, whereas the first three are definitive. This distinction is crucial for understanding the hierarchy of evidence and for refuting those who reject analogical reasoning entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening objection: why didn't the author simply say 'the principles of Sharia are four'? Understand the critique about listing qiyas separately.
The objection assumes qiyas should be listed equally, but the author chose a different wording.
2
Analyze the first answer
The author explains that the first three sources (Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma) are generally qat'i al-thubut (definitive in proof), while qiyas is generally zanni (speculative). Note that this is a general rule; some parts of Kitab/Sunnah are zanni, and some qiyas based on qat'i texts is qat'i.
This refutes the notion that qiyas is always speculative.
3
Analyze the second answer
The author's wording ('al-asl al-rabi al-qiyas') explicitly refutes deniers of qiyas. By mentioning it as a principle, he affirms its validity emphatically, not implicitly.
The separate mention also shows qiyas is subsidiary, used only when the first three sources do not provide a ruling.
4
Synthesize: hierarchy of sources
Combine both answers: qiyas is a source but with lower epistemic rank, and its explicit listing serves to both validate and subordinate it. This clarifies why the author did not simply say 'four'.
The passage defends the Hanafi position and refutes deniers by highlighting the deliberate wording.

Source Text

(والرابع ) کے ذکر سے ان پر صراحت اور ارادہ جزم کے ساتھ رو ہوا دوسری صورت میں ضمنار د حاصل ہو رہا تھا اس سے اس سوال کا بھی جواب ہو گیا کہ کتاب سنت اور اجماع کے ساتھ (الاصل) کا لفظ نہیں لائے اور قیاس میں کہا (الاصل الرابع القياس)؟ جواب ..... اس طرح مصنف رحمہ اللہ نے قیاس کا مرتبہ واضح کردیا کہ جب حکم کتاب، سنت اور اجماع میں نہ ملے تو قیاس کی طرف مراجعت کی جائیگی اگر حکم ابتدائی تین ادلہ واصول سے حاصل ہو جائے تو قیاس کی طرف کوئی حاجت نہیں۔ اشکال . کتاب سنت اجماع اور قیاس کو اصل کہنا صحیح نہیں کیونکہ کتاب اور سنت تصدیق باللہ کی
Objection: When qiyas is also an independent principle, why did the author (matn) not say: 'Know that the principles of Sharia are four'? First answer: The author intends to indicate that although qiyas is an independent principle, the ruling (hukm) of the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma is qat'i al-thubut (definitive in proof), while the ruling of qiyas is zanni (speculative). This is said considering the majority of rulings; otherwise, some types of the Book (like general texts subject to specification) and some types of Sunnah (like ahad hadith) are zanni, while a type of qiyas based on definitive text is qat'i. Second answer: The author adopted this style to refute the deniers of qiyas. [Further objection]: 'Then what benefit is there in mentioning 'al-rabi' separately?' Answer: By mentioning 'and the fourth', explicit and emphatic refutation is achieved; otherwise, implicit refutation would result. This also answers why the word 'al-asl' is not used with Kitab, Sunnah, and Ijma, but is used with qiyas as 'al-asl al-rabi' al-qiyas'. Answer: In this way, the author clarified the rank of qiyas: when a ruling is not found in Kitab, Sunnah, and Ijma, then one turns to qiyas. If the ruling is obtained from the first three proofs and principles, then there is no need for qiyas. Objection: It is not correct to call Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas 'principles' because the Book and Sunnah are...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses objections about why Qiyas is listed as the fourth principle and why it is called 'al-asl al-rabi'. The author explains that the first three sources (Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma) are generally qat'i (definitive) in their proof, whereas qiyas is generally zanni (speculative). However, this is a general rule; some parts of Kitab and Sunnah are zanni, and some qiyas based on definitive texts is qat'i. The separate mention is to refute those who reject qiyas entirely, making the refutation explicit. Moreover, by calling it 'the fourth principle', the author emphasizes that qiyas is to be consulted only when no ruling is found in the first three sources, showing its subsidiary rank.

Key Terms
قطعی الثبوت (qat'i al-thubut): Definitive in its authenticity: the Book, mutawatir Sunnah, and ijma are generally qat'i.
ظنی (zanni): Speculative: qiyas and ahad hadith are generally speculative in proof or indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author mention 'al-asl al-rabi' for qiyas but not 'al-asl' for the first three?
A) To show qiyas is more important
B) To indicate that qiyas is subsidiary to the first three
C) Because qiyas is not a real source
D) For brevity

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a valid source but with generally speculative proof; its separate mention emphasizes its subsidiary rank and refutes deniers.

Is the Basmala Part of the Quran? Hanafi Position and Proofs
Presenting three objections to basmala's Quranicity and the Hanafi rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the three objections against the basmala's Quranic status and evaluate the Hanafi responses using the concept of shubha (doubt).

Core Idea
Denial of the basmala does not lead to takfir because its Quranicity is subject to doubt (shubha).

This passage examines a key usul al-Fiqh dispute: whether the basmala (Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim) is a verse of the Quran. Some scholars, particularly commentators on al-Manar, argue that it is not, citing three proofs. The majority of Hanafis maintain it is part of the Quran and respond to each proof, using the principle of shubha to explain why its denier is not an unbeliever. By analyzing this dispute, you will learn how the Hanafi school navigates textual doubt and authoritative consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Determine what exactly is being debated: whether the basmala is a verse (ayat) of the Quran.
The term 'juz' al-Quran' means part of the Quran.
2
State the three proofs of the opponents
List the three arguments given by those who exclude basmala: no takfir for denial, basmala alone does not suffice for prayer, and junub may recite it.
These are based on the phrase 'bila shubha' in the Quran's definition.
3
Explain the Hanafi response to each proof
For each objection, provide the Hanafi counter-argument: (1) shubha prevents takfir; (2) basmala is not a complete verse according to some; (3) junub are allowed due to doubt.
The responses rely on the existence of legitimate scholarly doubt.
4
Understand the role of shubha
Analyze how the concept of shubha (uncertainty) prevents the application of takfir and other strict rulings.
Shubha is a key usul principle that protects from hasty judgments.
5
Conclude the practical consequence
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: how the dispute affects rulings like wudu for touching basmala, recitation in prayer, etc.
Hanafis treat basmala as Quran; opponents do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض اصول بین اور شارحین منار رحمہم اللہ تعالٰی نے کہا ہے کہ بلا شبهة کے ذریعہ مصنف رحمہ اللہ نے تسمیہ سے احتراز کیا ہے جس پر تین دلائل ہیں: پہلی دلیل: بسم اللہ تسمیہ کے منکر کی تکفیر نہیں کی گئی ہے حالانکہ قرآن کی ادنی سے ادنیٰ آیت کا منکر بالا جماع کافر ہے۔ دوسری دلیل .... اگر کوئی صرف بسم اللہ پڑھے تو فرض قراءت ادا نہیں ہوگی۔ تیسری دلیل .... جنبی مردوں عورتوں اور حیض و نفاس والی عورتوں کیلئے اس کا پڑھنا جائز ہے حالانکہ وہ تلاوت قرآن نہیں کر سکتے ۔ جمہور احناف کے ہاں بسم اللہ قرآن میں داخل ہے جہاں تک دلائل مذکورہ کا تعلق ہے ان کے جوابات دیئے گئے ہیں پہلی دلیل کا جواب تسمیہ کے منکر کی تکفیر اس لئے نہیں کی جاتی کیونکہ اسکے جزء قرآن ہونے میں شبہ ہے۔ دوسری دلیل کا جواب نفس تسمیہ فرض قراءة کے لئے اس لئے کافی نہیں کہ یہ بعض کے ہاں مکمل آیت نہیں ہے۔
Some scholars of usul and commentators of al-Manar (may Allah have mercy on them) said that through 'bila shubha' the author (may Allah have mercy on him) intended to exclude the basmala (tasmiya). For this there are three proofs: The first proof: The denier of the basmala (tasmiya) is not declared an unbeliever (takfir), whereas the denier of the least verse of the Quran is an unbeliever by consensus. The second proof: If someone recites only 'Bismillah', the obligatory recital (in prayer) is not fulfilled. The third proof: It is permissible for junub men, women, and menstruating or post-natal women to recite it, while they cannot recite the Quran. According to the majority of Hanafis, the basmala is included in the Quran. As for the mentioned proofs, they have been answered: The answer to the first proof is that the denier of the basmala is not declared an unbeliever because there is doubt (shubha) about its being a part of the Quran. The answer to the second proof: The basmala itself is not sufficient for the obligatory recital because according to some it is not a complete verse.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding whether the basmala ('Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim') is part of the Quran. Some scholars (identified as 'usuliyyin and commentators of al-Manar') argue that the phrase 'bila shubha' in the Quran's definition is meant to exclude the basmala, because its inclusion is not certain. They provide three proofs: (1) Denying the basmala does not lead to takfir, while denying any other verse does; (2) Reciting only the basmala does not fulfill the obligatory recitation in prayer; (3) Junub and menstruating individuals can recite the basmala but not Quran. The majority of Hanafis, however, maintain that the basmala is part of the Quran. They respond: (a) The doubt about its inclusion (whether it is a complete verse or opening of a sura) prevents takfir; (b) The basmala is not considered a complete verse for the purpose of prayer by those who deny it; therefore, it does not fulfill the obligation. The passage underscores the Hanafi commitment to the basmala's Qur'anic status while acknowledging scholarly differences.

Key Terms
تسمیہ (tasmiya): The act of saying 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' (the basmala).
جزء قرآن (juz' al-Quran): A part of the Quranic text; specifically whether the basmala is one of its verses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the status of the basmala?
A) It is not part of the Quran.
B) It is part of the Quran, and its denier becomes an unbeliever.
C) It is part of the Quran, but its denier is not declared an unbeliever due to doubt about its verse status.
D) It is part of the Quran only in Surah al-Fatiha.

Key Takeaway
The basmala's Quranic status is affirmed by the Hanafi school, but the presence of doubt (shubha) prevents takfir of those who deny it, illustrating the Hanafi principle of avoiding hasty judgments in cases of uncertainty.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

10 min
Tahlil and the Count of Divorces: Hadith vs. Quranic Verse
Dispute on whether tahlil resets the count of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the number of divorces after tahlil and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on Quran 2:230.

Core Idea
Tahlil resets the divorce count to three based on hadith, not on the Quranic verse.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: when a woman divorced fewer than three times undergoes tahlil and remarries her first husband, does the number of divorces reset? The answer depends on whether the Quranic verse or the hadith of al-'Usaylah governs this case. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to weigh textual evidence across madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Determine the exact situation: a husband gives one or two divorces (not three), then tahlil occurs, and the couple remarries.
The consensus exists for triple divorce; the dispute is for lesser counts.
2
State the opposing positions
Imam Muhammad and Imam Shafi'i hold that only the remaining divorces are available; Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf hold that all three divorces become available again.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
3
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Imam Shafi'i uses Quran 2:230 – the word 'hatta' (until) indicates a limit that only removes the prohibition for triple divorce; it does not grant a new set of divorces for lesser counts.
They argue that the verse's exception (mughayya) applies only to the case it mentions (three divorces).
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis do not rely on the verse but on the hadith of al-'Usaylah. The Prophet's silence and the hadith's implication (isharat al-nass) indicate that the second husband makes the woman lawful anew with a fresh marriage.
This hadith is also used by Shafi'i to require a wali, but Hanafis use it for the reset of divorces.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text sides with the Hanafi response, emphasizing that the hadith directly addresses the situation, whereas the Quranic verse only covers the triple-divorce case.
In Hanafi usul, a specific hadith (khas) can qualify a general Quranic verse (amm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ومحللية الزوج الثانى بحديث العسيلة لا بقوله حتى تنكح زوجا غيره .
امام شافعی کی طرف سے ہم پر دو اعتراض ہوتے ہیں۔
(1) مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ جوشخص اپنی بیوی کو تین طلاقیں دیدے اور وہ حلالہ کرنے کے بعد دوبارہ پہلے شوہر کے ساتھ نکاح کرے تو بالاتفاق وہ تین طلاقوں کا مالک ہو جائیگا۔ لیکن اگر پہلے شوہر نے ایک یا دو طلاقیں دیدیں اور حلالے کے بعد دوبارہ اس کے ساتھ نکاح ہوا۔ تو امام محمد اور امام شافعی رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک تین طلاقوں میں سے جو بچ گئی ہیں۔ زوج اول کو صرف انہیں کا اختیار ہوگا اور امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام یوسف کے نزدیک اسے تین طلاقوں کا اختیار ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ زوج ثانی نے اس عورت کو زوج اول کیلئے نئے سرے سے حلال کر دیا ہے یعنی حل جدید کیساتھ حلال کر دیا ۔ لہذا پہلے نکاح میں جو طلاق ہوئی تھی اس کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہوگا ۔
تمہیدی کلمات کے بعد اب اصل اعتراض کی طرف آئیے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ نے اس پر اعتراض کیا کہ حلالہ کے بارے میں جو آیت ہے۔ فان طلقها فلا تحل له من بعد حتى تنكح زوجا غيرة اس میں لفظ حتی خاص ہے غایت اور انتہاء کے معنی کے ساتھ یعنی اس سے اتنا معلوم ہوا کہ زوج ثانی کیساتھ نکاح کرنے سے پہلی والی حرمت ختم ہو جائے گی لیکن اس سے یہ معلوم نہیں آتا کہ وہ عورت زوج اول کیلئے حل جدید کیساتھ حلال ہوگی۔ تو جس صورت میں مغیا پایا جاتا ہے یعنی تین طلاقیں جب اس میں زوج ثانی زوج اول کے لئے بالاتفاق محلل نہیں بن سکتا تو جہاں مغیا نہیں پایا جاتا وہاں وہ کیسے محلل بنے گا۔
اعتراض مذکور کا احناف کی طرف سے جواب ..... امام ابو حنیفہ کی طرف سے مصنف نے یہ جواب دیا که زوج ثانی کے محلل ہونے کو ہم ( حتی تنكح ) سے ثابت نہیں کرتے بلکہ ہم اس کو حدیث عسیلہ سے ثابت کرتے ہیں۔ یہ حدیث مشہور ہے اور اس حدیث سے امام شافعی بھی حلالے کے لئے ولی کی شرط کو ثابت کرتے ہیں تو ہم نے کہا کہ اس حدیث کی ولالہ ان سے ولی کی شرط ثابت ہوتی ہے اسی طرح اس کے اشارۃ النص سے زوج ثانی کا محلل ہونا ثابت ہوتا ہے اس لئے کہ اللہ کے رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے امراة رفاعہ سے یہ نہیں کہا
His saying: 'And the second husband is a muhallil (legalizer) based on the hadith of al-'Usaylah, not based on His saying 'until she marries another husband'.' We receive two objections from Imam Shafi'i. (1) The issue is: If a person gives his wife three divorces and after tahlil she remarries the first husband, by consensus he will own three divorces. But if the first husband gave one or two divorces and after tahlil she remarries him, then according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Shafi'i, he will only have the remaining number of divorces. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, he will have the right to three divorces, because the second husband made her lawful for the first husband anew, i.e., with a new halal. So the divorces from the first marriage are not considered. Now the actual objection: Imam Shafi'i argued regarding the verse 'Fa in tallaqaha fala tahillu lahu min ba'du hatta tankaha zawjan ghayrah' that the word 'hatta' indicates a limit and end: that the prohibition ends upon marriage to the second husband, but it does not indicate that she becomes lawful with a new halal. Thus, where the exception (mughayya) is found (i.e., in the case of three divorces), the second husband is unanimously not a muhallil for the first husband; how then can he be a muhallil in a case where the exception is not found? The response from the Hanafis: The author gave the answer on behalf of Abu Hanifa that we do not prove that the second husband is a muhallil from 'hatta tankaha', but we prove it from the hadith of al-'Usaylah. This was the statement of the wife of Rif'ah? ...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a legal dispute (usul dispute) concerning the effect of tahlil (intermediate marriage) on the number of divorces a first husband can exercise upon remarriage. The core issue: If a man divorces his wife fewer than three times (e.g., one or two), and she marries another man and then returns to the first husband, how many divorces does he possess? According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam al-Shafi'i, he only has the remaining divorces (e.g., if he gave two, he now only has one left). According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, he gains full three divorces again because the second marriage resets the count entirely. The passage then details Imam Shafi'i's objection: the Quranic verse 'until she marries another husband' only removes the prohibition for triple divorce cases, but does not imply a new starting point for a lesser number. The Hanafi response is that they do not rely on the verse for this rule; instead, they cite the hadith of 'Uwaymir (al-Usaylah) in which the Prophet did not tell the woman something (the text is cut off, but the hadith indicates that the second husband makes her lawful for the first with a new halal). This hadith, they argue, by its implication (isharat al-nass), proves that the second husband is a muhallil and that the marriage is a fresh start.

Key Terms
حلالہ (tahlil): The process by which a woman who has been divorced three times marries another man and is divorced by him, making her lawful for her first husband.
محلل (muhallil): The second husband who, by his marriage and subsequent divorce, makes the woman lawful for her first husband.
حديث العسيلة (hadith al-'usaylah): A hadith about a woman named 'Uwaymir (or al-Usaylah) who complained about her second husband, and the Prophet (PBUH) did not forbid her from returning to the first husband, indicating that the second husband is a muhallil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why does the second husband count as a muhallil even when the first husband gave fewer than three divorces?
A) Because the Quranic verse explicitly says so
B) Because of consensus among all scholars
C) Because of the hadith of al-'Usaylah
D) Because of qiyas with the triple divorce case

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that tahlil creates a new marriage, resetting the divorce count to three, based on the specific implication of the hadith of al-'Usaylah, not on the general Quranic verse.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Fifth Branch: Validity of Talaq after Khul'
Refutation of Shafi'i objection using Quranic verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi istinbat method using the letter 'fa' to derive the ruling on talaq after khul' and critically evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The letter 'fa' in Quran 2:229 proves that a valid talaq can follow khul'.

This lesson delves into a classic usul dispute: can a husband pronounce a valid talaq after a khul' divorce? The Hanafi school says yes, based on the sequential structure of the Quranic verses. The Shafi'i school disagrees, reading the verses differently. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to handle 'amm' and 'khass' and to appreciate how linguistic features like 'fa' shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the contested issue and the two positions
Identify the exact question: is a talaq pronounced after khul' valid? State the Hanafi position (yes, it is valid and counts as the third talaq) and the Shafi'i position (no, because khul' dissolves the marriage).
Remember the practical consequence: after khul' in Hanafi, the husband can still give a third talaq making the woman haram; in Shafi'i, no further talaq is possible.
2
Identify the Hanafi dalil and its linguistic feature
Locate the Quranic evidence: Surah al-Baqarah 2:229-230. Note the sequence: 'Divorce is twice' → rules of khul' → 'then if he divorces her' (the third talaq). The key linguistic feature is the letter 'fa' (ف) in 'فإن طلقها' which indicates immediate sequence.
Hanafi usul holds that 'fa' is for sequence (ta'qib). Shafi'i usul treats it as a mere conjunction with no necessary sequence in this context.
3
Trace the Hanafi istinbat chain
Work through the derivation: (1) The verse specifies that revocable divorces are limited to two (takhsis). (2) Khul' is mentioned as a permissible separation after those two. (3) The 'fa' before the third talaq shows that it occurs after khul', making talaq after khul' valid. This yields the ruling that the husband can give the third talaq after khul'.
Note that Hanafis consider khul' itself a type of talaq subsumed within the two, so no fourth talaq arises.
4
Examine the Shafi'i counter-reading
The Shafi'i school argues that the mention of khul' is a parenthetical clause. The third talaq ('if he divorces her') connects back to 'divorce is twice', not to the khul' verses. They view the sequence as: two divorces → possibility of khul' (which dissolves marriage) → no further talaq after khul'.
Shafi'is interpret 'tasrih bi ihsan' (release with kindness) as the third talaq, not as part of the khul' context.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the preferred position
The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, a man can give a third talaq after khul', making the woman prohibited until she marries another. In the Shafi'i school, the marriage is already dissolved by khul', so no further talaq occurs. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) due to the apparent sequence of the Quran.
Both positions are legitimate scholarly views; the choice rests on usul principles regarding 'fa' and parenthetical clauses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ہماری طرف سے جواب ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ (فا) لفظ خاص ہے یہ معنی مخصوص کیلئے وضع کیا گیا ہے۔ اور وہ تعقیب ہے تو جب اس طلاق کو خلع کے بعد ذکر کیا گیا تو خلع کے بعد بھی طلاق جائز ہونی چاہیے۔

اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اس طرح تو چار طلاقیں ہو جائینگی۔

جواب خلع کوئی مستقل طلاق نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ ان ہی دو طلاقوں سے ضمن میں ہے گو یا اللہ تعالیٰ نے یوں فرمایا ہے کہ طلاقیں دو ہیں خواہ وہ دونوں رجعی ہوں یا دونوں خلع کے ضمن میں یعنی بائنہ ہوں پھر ان دو طلاقوں کے بعد اگر طلاق دیگا تو عورت اس کے لئے حلال نہیں ہیگی ۔

اعتراض .... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اس سے یہ لازم آتا ہے کہ عدم احلال کا حکم صرف اسی طلاق کا ہو جو خلع کے بعد ہے حالانکہ یہ میچ نہیں ہے۔

جواب ..... جواب یہ ہے کہ حلالہ کا حکم اس طلاق کا ہے جو دو طلاقوں کے بعد آئی ہو خواہ وہ دونوں طلاقیں خلع کے ضمن میں ہوں یا نہ ہوں۔

اعتراض ایک اور اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ یہاں سے لازم آتا ہے کہ خلع من دو طلاقوں کے بعد جائز ہے فان خفتم اس پر دلالت کرتا ہے۔ کیونکہ فاء تعقیبیہ ہے اور الطلاق مرتان کے بعد ہے كما قال الله تعالى فان خفتم الا يقيما حدود الله ..... الخ

جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم نے بتلا دیا کہ خلع کوئی مستقل طلاق نہیں ہے یہ ان دو طلاقوں کے ضمن میں بھی آسکتی ہے۔ اور ان کے بغیر بھی آسکتی ہے۔ مگر یہ ساری تفصیل اس وقت ہے جب ہم یہ کہیں کہ تسریح باحسان سے مراد ترک مراجعت ہے لیکن اگر تسریح باحسان سے تیسری طلاق کی طرف اشارہ ہو تو پھر فان طلقھا اس کا بیان ہوگا اور خلع کے مسئلے کے ساتھ اس کا کچھ تعلق نہیں ہو گا۔ مزید تفصیل کتاب کے حاشیہ میں ملاحظہ فرمائیں۔
Note: After mentioning several objections from the Shafi'is on the fourth branch and their answers, Allamah Abdullah Nasli is presenting the fifth branch on the ruling of the specific (matn).

His statement: And that is the validity of effecting divorce after khul'.

Fifth branch: The author raises the fifth branch on the ruling of the specific; this requires a preface.

Prefatory words: Imam al-Shafi'i says that khul' annuls the marriage. Therefore, divorce cannot occur after khul'. But we say that khul' is not an annulment of marriage; rather, like other divorces, it is also a divorce. Hence, divorce can occur after khul'. Our evidence is from this verse of the Noble Quran: 'And if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until...' etc. Because here it was first said: 'Divorce is twice' i.e., two revocable divorces. Then after that, the issue of khul' was mentioned: 'If you fear that they will not maintain the limits of Allah.' So from this it is understood that in khul' the woman's part is to pay ransom and the man's part is to divorce. After khul', the third divorce was mentioned, so it is known that divorce can occur after khul'. The Shafi'is' answer: Imam al-Shafi'i says that 'and if he divorces her' etc. is connected to 'divorce is twice', and the mention of khul' in between is a parenthetical clause.

Our response: Our evidence is that the letter 'fa' is specific; it is placed for a specific meaning, and that is sequence. So when this divorce was mentioned after khul', then divorce must be permissible after khul'.

Objection: An objection is raised that in this way four divorces would occur.

Answer: Khul' is not an independent divorce; rather, it is included within those two divorces. It is as if Allah said: Divorces are two, whether both are revocable or both are within the scope of khul' i.e., irrevocable. Then after these two divorces, if he divorces, the woman will not be lawful for him.

Objection: Another objection is that this entails that the ruling of non-permissibility applies only to the divorce that comes after khul', even though this is not correct.

Answer: The answer is that the ruling of non-permissibility applies to the divorce that comes after two divorces, whether those two divorces are within the scope of khul' or not.

Objection: Another objection is that this entails that khul' is permissible after two divorces, and 'if you fear' indicates that, because 'fa' is sequential and comes after 'divorce is twice', as Allah said: 'If you fear that they will not maintain the limits of Allah...' etc.

Answer: The answer is that we have stated that khul' is not an independent divorce; it can be included within those two divorces or without them. But all this detail applies when we say that 'release with kindness' means abandonment of return. But if 'release with kindness' refers to the third divorce, then 'and if he divorces her' is its explanation and it has no connection to the issue of khul'. See further detail in the margin of the book.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on the validity of talaq (divorce) after khul' (divorce at wife's initiative with compensation). The Shafi'i position is that khul' is a dissolution (faskh) of marriage, not a divorce, so no further talaq can occur after it. The Hanafi position, presented here, is that khul' is itself a type of talaq, counted within the two permissible divorces mentioned in the Quran. The author begins by stating the matn: that it is valid to effect talaq after khul'. He then provides a preface explaining the dispute. The Hanafi evidence is the Quranic verse sequence: first 'divorce is twice' (al-Baqarah 2:229), then the rules of khul', then 'if he divorces her' (the third divorce). The use of the letter 'fa' indicates sequence, proving that khul' is followed by talaq. The Shafi'is respond that the verse about the third divorce is actually connected to the first part ('divorce is twice'), and the khul' mention is parenthetical. The Hanafis rebut that 'fa' denotes immediate sequence, so the third divorce must occur after khul'. The passage also addresses objections: if khul' is a divorce, wouldn't there be four divorces? The Hanafi answer is that khul' is not an independent third divorce but is subsumed within the first two; it can be either revocable or irrevocable. Another objection is that the ruling of non-permissibility (of remarriage without tahlil) would only apply to the talaq after khul', which is illogical. The Hanafis clarify that the ruling applies to any third talaq after two divorces, irrespective of whether they were on a khul' basis. A final objection concerns the interpretation of 'tasrih bi ihsan' (release with kindness); the author notes that if it refers to the third divorce, then the issue is separate. This passage is an expansion of the matn's concise ruling, typical of a sharh.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): A form of separation initiated by the wife by paying compensation to the husband, considered a type of talaq (Hanafi) or dissolution (Shafi'i).
فاء تعقیبیہ (fa' ta'qibiyya): The letter 'fa' used to indicate immediate sequence or succession between two events.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for the validity of talaq after khul'?
A) The hadith of the Prophet on khul'.
B) The sequential indication of the letter 'fa' in Quran 2:229-230.
C) Ijma' of the Companions.
D) Qiyas on regular talaq.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that talaq after khul' is valid is based on the sequential indication of the letter 'fa' in the Quran, and it has practical consequences for divorce counts and marital status.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
7 min
Hanafi Response to Objections on Halala and Khul'
Response to objections about halala after two divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi response to two objections regarding the ruling of halala after two divorces and identify the interpretive principle regarding the term 'tasrih bi-ihsan'.

Core Idea
Halala after two divorces applies regardless of iddah; khul' is not a separate divorce.

This lesson examines a key passage from Sharh Noor al-Anwar where the author addresses two objections concerning the ruling of halala (lawfulness for remarriage) after two divorces. The discussion hinges on the interpretation of the Quranic phrase 'tasrih bi-ihsan' and its relation to the waiting period ('idl) and khul'. Understanding the Hanafi response clarifies important usul principles about the derivation of rulings from the Quran and the handling of apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
First, the objection that the ruling of non-halala applies only to a divorce after 'idl; second, the objection that khul' is permissible after two divorces based on the verse. Read the objections carefully in the text.
The objections are hypothetical but represent common misunderstandings.
2
State the author's responses
For the first objection, the author clarifies that halala applies to the divorce after two divorces regardless of 'idl. For the second, he explains that khul' is not an independent divorce but can be included within the two divorces. Summarize both responses.
3
Analyze the use of the verse 'fain khiftum'
The second objection uses the sequential 'fa' to argue that khul' occurs after two divorces. The author counters by explaining that khul' is subsumed under the two divorces. Identify the Hanafi usul principle at work: the particle 'fa' indicates sequence but does not necessarily create an independent category.
This illustrates the Hanafi approach to istidlal (inference) from the Quran.
4
Examine the role of 'tasrih bi-ihsan'
The author notes that the entire discussion depends on the interpretation of 'tasrih bi-ihsan'. If it means refraining from returning (i.e., not revoking the divorce), then the issue of 'idl is separate. If it means the third divorce, then the relationship changes. Distinguish the two interpretations.
This shows the importance of lexical analysis in usul.
5
Derive the final Hanafi ruling
Based on the responses, state the Hanafi ruling: After two divorces, any additional divorce (whether with or without iddah) renders the woman unlawful for the husband unless she marries another and is divorced (halala). Khul' does not count as a separate divorce for this purpose.
This is the practical outcome.

ضمن میں یعنی بائنہ ہوں پھر ان دو طلاقوں کے بعد اگر طلاق دیگا تو عورت اس کے لئے حلال نہیں ہیگی ۔ اعتراض .... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اس سے یہ لازم آتا ہے کہ عدم احلال کا حکم صرف اسی طلاق کا ہو جو ضلع کے بعد ہے حالانکہ یہ میچ نہیں ہے۔ جواب ..... جواب یہ ہے کہ حلالہ کا حکم اس طلاق کا ہے جو دو طلاقوں کے بعد آئی ہو خواہ وہ دونوں طلاقیں ضلع سے ضمن میں ہوں یا نہ ہوں۔ اعتراض ایک اور اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ یہاں سے لازم آتا ہے کہ خلع من دو طلاقوں کے بعد جائز ہے فان خفتم اس پر دلالت کرتا ہے۔ کیونکہ فاء تعقیبیہ ہے اور الطلاق مرتان کے بعد ہے كما قال الله تعالى فان خفتم الا يقيما حدود الله ..... الخ جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم نے بتلا دیا کہ خلع کوئی مستقل طلاق نہیں ہے یہ ان دو طلاقوں کے ضمن میں بھی آسکتی ہے۔ اور ان کے بغیر بھی آسکتی ہے۔ مگر یہ ساری تفصیل اس وقت ہے جب ہم یہ کہیں کہ تشریح باحسان سے مراد ترک مراجعت ہے لیکن اگر تسریح باحسان سے تیسری طلاق کی طرف اشارہ ہو تو پھر فان طلقھا اس کا بیان ہوگا اور ضلع کے مسئلے کے ساتھ اس کا کچھ تعلق نہیں ہو گا۔ مزید تفصیل کتاب کے حاشیہ میں ملاحظہ فرمائیں۔
Within that is, i.e., ba'in divorces, then after those two divorces, if he divorces, the woman will not be halal for him. Objection... Objection is that from this it follows that the ruling of non-halala applies only to that divorce which is after 'idl, while this is not correct. Answer... The answer is that the ruling of halala applies to that divorce which comes after two divorces, whether those two divorces are with 'idl or without. Another objection is that from here it follows that khul' is permissible after two divorces, as 'fain khiftum' indicates this, because 'fa' is for sequence, and it is after 'al-talaqu marratan' as Allah said: 'fain khiftum alla yuqima hududa Allah' ... etc. The answer is that we have explained that khul' is not an independent divorce; it can come within those two divorces or without them. But all this detail is when we say that 'tasrih bi-ihsan' means leaving off returning, but if 'tasrih bi-ihsan' refers to the third divorce, then 'fain tallaqaha' is its explanation, and it will have no connection with the issue of 'idl. For more detail, see the margin of the book.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that addresses two objections regarding the ruling of three divorces. The first objection questions the implication that the ruling of being unlawful (ihlal) is restricted to the divorce that occurs after the waiting period ('idl). The author responds by clarifying that the ruling applies to the divorce after two divorces regardless of whether an 'idl is involved. The second objection arises from the verse 'fain khiftum' (2:229) and the sequence of the word 'fa'. The author explains that khul' is not a separate divorce but can be included within the two divorces. Finally, the author notes that this interpretation depends on whether 'tasrih bi-ihsan' refers to refraining from returning (i.e., not revoking the divorce) or to the third divorce itself.

Key Terms
تسریح باحسان (tasrih bi-ihsan): Releasing kindly; interpreted either as refraining from taking back the wife (leaving her without revocation) or as giving the third divorce.
خلع (khul'): A divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation; here it is clarified that khul' is not an independent divorce but can be subsumed under the two divorces mentioned in the verse.

Key Takeaway
After two divorces, any additional divorce renders the woman unlawful for the husband, and khul' is not a separate divorce in this context.

Hanafi-Shafiʻi Dispute on the Meaning of 'Farḍ'
Dispute over the interpretation of 'farḍ' in Quran 33:50

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two textual indicators cited by Imam al-Shafi'i for the obligatory meaning of 'farḍ' in Quran 33:50, and explain the Hanafi response using the concept of an implied second verb.

Core Idea
When two scholars read the same verse, the difference lies in the presence or absence of an implied verb.

The word 'farḍ' carries both a linguistic meaning of 'determination' and a technical fiqh meaning of 'obligation'. In this lesson, we examine how Imam al-Shafi'i and the Hanafis argued over which meaning is intended in Quran 33:50. The dispute turns on two linguistic indicators and whether an implied second verb resolves the apparent contradiction. Understanding this debate sharpens your skill in weighing qarā'in (contextual clues) in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and the contested word
The verse is Quran 33:50, specifically the phrase 'قد علمنا ما فرضنا عليهم' (We have indeed prescribed for them). The word 'faradna' (from farḍ) is in dispute. Do you know the literal meaning of farḍ?
Literal meaning includes 'determination' (تقدير) and 'cutting' (قطع); technical means 'obligation'.
2
State Imam al-Shafi'i's position and his two indicators
Al-Shafi'i argues that 'farḍ' here means 'obligation'. He provides two qarā'in: (1) the verb is transitive with 'ala', which is the pattern for obligating; (2) the conjunctive structure 'أزواجهم وما ملكت أيمانهم' implies maintenance is obligatory for both wives and slave women. How does the conjunctive support his view?
The second indicator uses the fact that slave women have no dower, only maintenance, so the verb must refer to obligating maintenance.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond by positing an implied second 'faradna' before 'ما ملكت أيمانهم'. The first 'faradna' (with 'ala') means 'we determined' (قدرنا), and the implied one means 'we obligated' (أوجبنا). This resolves the grammatical issue without requiring 'farḍ' to mean obligation in the first occurrence. Why does the Hanafi response preserve the linguistic meaning?
The use of 'ala' is explained by the fact that the verb still carries a nuance of obligation in this context, but the primary meaning remains determination.
4
Locate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The disagreement affects the ruling on maintenance for slave women. Since the Hanafis take the first 'faradna' as determination, they derive that maintenance becomes obligatory via the second implied verb. Shafi'is, taking the whole as obligation, may derive a different ruling. What is the practical difference?
This is a real fiqh difference, not just a theoretical exercise.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderance)
The author of the text (Al-Hidayah or similar) presents the Hanafi response as the final word, indicating that the mufta bihi (operative opinion) is the Hanafi position. Why might the Hanafi reading be considered stronger?
The ability to explain both indicators without changing the primary meaning of 'farḍ' gives it internal coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آگے صاحب کتاب نے وضاحت فرمائی ہے کہ (فرض) کے معنی کے ضمن میں جو کچھ ہم نے بیان کیا ہے وه فقها ، کی اصطلاح میں ہے جبکہ لغت میں فرض کا معنی ہے ایجاب اور قطع ۔ اور امام شافعی فر ماتے ہیں کہ یہاں ایجاب والا معنی مراد ہے اور اس پر دو قرینے ہیں ایک تو یہ کہ فرض کو متعدی کیا علی کے ذریعے سے کنا قال تعالى قد علمنا ما غر ضنا علیہم اور قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ فرض جب متعدی ہولی کے ساتھ تو اس سے ایجاب والا معنی مراد ہوتا ہے۔ دوسرا قرینہ یہ ہے کہ مملکت ايمانهم كا عطف ( ازواجهم) پر ہے اور لونڈیوں کیلئے تو مہر ہے ہی نہیں بلکہ نفقہ ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ یہاں مراد یہ ہے کہ نفقہ واجب ہے بیویوں کے لئے بھی اور لونڈیوں کے لئے بھی ۔ جو یا قد علمنا ما فرضنا کا تعلق وجوب مهر سے نہیں بلکہ وجوب نفقہ سے ہے۔ احناف کا جواب ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ علی کے ذریعے سے اس وجہ سے متعدی ہے کہ فرض یہاں پر ایجاب کے معنی کو ضمن ہے اور ملکت ايمانهم سے پہلے دوسرا فرضنا مقدر ہے تو پہلے فرضنا کا معنی ہے قدرنا اور دوسرے فرضنا کا معنی ہے او جنا ۔
Further, the author of the book has clarified that what we have explained regarding the meaning of 'farḍ' is in the terminology of fiqh, whereas in language, the meaning of 'farḍ' is obligation and cutting. Imam Shafi'i says that here the meaning of obligation is intended, and he has two indicators: first, that 'farḍ' is made transitive with 'ala', as in the verse 'We have indeed prescribed (faradna) for them' – and the rule is that when 'farḍ' is transitive with 'ala', it means obligation. Second, that 'the spouses they possess' is conjuncted to 'their spouses', and for slave women there is no dower, rather there is maintenance, so it is known that here the meaning is that maintenance is obligatory for both wives and slave women. So the verse 'We have indeed prescribed' relates not to the obligation of dower but to the obligation of maintenance. The Hanafis respond: It is made transitive with 'ala' because 'farḍ' here implies the meaning of obligation, and before 'the spouses they possess' there is an implied second 'faradna', so the first 'faradna' means 'we determined' and the second 'faradna' means 'we obligated'.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the interpretation of the word 'farḍ' (فرض) in the Quranic verse (33:50). Imam Shafi'i argues that 'farḍ' here means 'obligation' based on two indicators: the transitive use with 'ala' and the conjunctive structure of the verse. The Hanafis respond by positing an implied second 'faradna', distinguishing between 'farḍ' as determination (تقدیر) and as obligation (ایجاب). This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul to maintain that the primary meaning of 'farḍ' in the verse is not obligation but determination, preserving their own interpretation of the ruling on maintenance for slave women.

Key Terms
فرض (farḍ): A term with dual meaning: in fiqh terminology, it denotes an obligatory ruling; in linguistic origin, it means 'determination' or 'cutting'.
قرینہ (qarīnah): An indicator or contextual clue that helps determine the intended meaning of a word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the meaning of the first 'faradna' in the implied verb structure?
A) Obligation (إيجاب)
B) Determination (تقدير)
C) Cutting (قطع)
D) Maintenance (نفقة)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi reading of 'farḍ' as 'determination' in Quran 33:50 demonstrates the principle that literal meaning prevails unless a decisive qarīnah shifts it, and any necessary obligation can be derived via an implied verb.

Does the Prophet's Action Indicate Obligation? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on fi'l al-nabi indicating wujub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on fi'l al-nabi, state the Hanafi evidence from the hadith of continuous fasting and sandals, evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument from the hadith 'Pray as you see me praying', and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The Prophet's action alone does not oblige; only a verbal command does.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: whether the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) themselves create a legal obligation (wujub). The Hanafi school holds that without a verbal command, his actions are not binding, while the Shafi'i school argues that his actions are inherently indicative of obligation. By analyzing the key evidence from both sides, you will understand how each school derives rulings from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold regarding fi'l al-nabi?
The Hanafi position is that the Prophet's action alone does not indicate obligation.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school objects and on what grounds?
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the Prophet's action indicates obligation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is their dalil? (Hadith 'Pray as you see me praying' and the Quranic verse)
The Shafi'i evidence includes the hadith 'صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي' and the verse 'ما أمر فرعون برشيد'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut this? (Distinguish command from action, metaphorical usage)
The Hanafis argue that the obligation comes from the verbal command, not the action, and the Quranic usage is metaphorical.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings? (For Hanafis, silent actions are mandub, not wajib; for Shafi'is, they are wajib unless evidence to contrary)
This difference affects how we view the Prophet's habits and practices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشافعي امام شافعی کا نہ ہ... امام محمد بن ادریس الشافعی کے نزدیک جیسے وجوب امر سے سمجھ میں آتا ہے اس طرح فعل رسول صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم سے بھی سمجھ میں آتا ہے مگر ہمارے نزدیک فقہ افعل رسول اللہ صلی علیہ وسلم سے وجوب سمجھ میں نہیں آتا ہے یہ اختلاف صرف ایسے افعال میں ہے جو نہ تو آپ سے کہوا ہوئے ہوں اور نہ طبعا ہوئے ہوں اور نہ ہی آپ کیسا تھ مخصوص ہوں ۔ مثلا د جوب تجد و غیره که وہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے ساتھ خاص ہے۔
قوله : للمنع عن الوصال وخلع الفعال احناف کی دلیل ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضور اکرم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کو مسلسل روزے رکھتے ہوئے دیکھ کر صحابہ نے بھی رکھنے شروع کر دیئے تو حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے منع فرمادیا اور فرمایا کہ ايكم مثلی یطعمنی ربی ویسقینی ؟ تم میں سے کون میری طرح ہو سکتا ہے مجھے تو دوام صوم کے باوجود بھی اللہ رب العزة کھلاتا اور پلاتا ہے ) مراد یہ تھی کہ تم صوم داؤدی پرعمل کرو میرے فعل کی اتباع تم پر واجب نہیں ہے۔۔
اس طرح حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ایک مرتبہ نماز میں اپنے جوتے مبارک اتار دیئے تو صحابہ رضی اللہ عنہم نے بھی اتارنا شروع کر دیئے تو حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا تم نے ایسا کیوں کیا اور اپ نزع نعل کی وجہ بھی بیان کر دی کہ میرے جوتوں میں کوئی چیز لگی ہوئی تھی۔ اگر ایسا ہو تو تم بھی اتار دیا کرو ورنہ اتارنا ضروری نہیں ہے اگر حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا. فعل وجوب کے لئے ہوتا آپ صحابہ رضی اللہ عنہم کو منع نہ فرماتے۔ لہذا معلوم ہواکر محض فعل نبی بدون امر موجب وجوب نہیں ہے۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل ... امام شافعی کبھی علی سبیل التنزل کہتے ہیں کہ فعل اگر چہ بعینہ امر نہیں لیکن فائدہ دینے میں امر کی طرح ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یوم خندق میں جب آپ صلی علیہ وسلم کی چار نمازیں قضا ہو میں تو جب آپ نے قضاء کی تو فرمایا (صلوا كمار أيتموني اصلی )
قوله : والوجوب استفيد بقوله عليه السلام صلوا كمار أيتموني اصلي
شوافع کو ہمارا جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ اگر آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا فعل وجوب کا فائدہ دیتا تو پھر آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کو صلو اكمار أيتموني ..... الخ کہنے کی ضرورت پیش نہ آتی کو بلکہ صحابہ آپ کے فعل کو دیکھ کرہی اس پر عمل پیرا ہو جاتے ۔ معلوم ہوا کہ وجوب کا فائدہ صلوا كما ...... الخ یعنی قول (امر) سے حاصل ہوا ہے نہ کہ فعل ہے۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل دیگر کبھی امام شافعی علی سبیل اترقی کہتے ہیں کہ فعل بھی امر کی ایک قسم ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ و ما امر فرعون بر شید میں فعل پر امر کا اطلاق ہوا ہے کیونکہ رشید فعل کی صفت بنتی ہے قول کی صفت نہیں بنتی۔ بلکہ قول کی صفت سدید ہوا کرتی ہے۔
قوله : سمّى الفعل به لأنه سببه
شوافع کو ہمارا جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ امر سبب ہے اور فعل مسبب ۔ اور یہ تسمیۃ السبب باسم المسبب کی قبیل سے ہے۔ الغرض یہاں سبب بول کر مسبب مراد لیا ہے جب کہ ہم حقیقت کی بات کرتے ہیں اور آپ نے مجاز سے استدلال کیا ہے جو قابل قبول نہیں۔
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that just as obligation is understood from command, so it is understood from the action of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). But according to us (Hanafis), obligation is not understood from the action of the Messenger of Allah. This difference is only regarding actions that are not spoken by him, nor done instinctively, nor specific to him. For example [؟] and others that are specific to the Prophet.
His statement: 'Prohibition of continuous fasting and removing sandals' — The Hanafi evidence: Our evidence is that when the Companions saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) continuously fasting, they also began to fast. The Prophet forbade them and said: 'Which of you is like me? My Lord feeds me and gives me drink.' The meaning was that you should follow the fast of Dawud; following my action is not obligatory upon you.
Similarly, once the Prophet removed his blessed sandals during prayer, and the Companions also began to remove theirs. The Prophet said: 'Why did you do that?' and explained the reason for removing his sandals: 'There was something stuck in my sandals. If such happens, you may remove them; otherwise, it is not necessary to remove them.' If the Prophet's action indicated obligation, he would not have forbidden them. Thus it is known that mere action of the Prophet without a command is not indicative of obligation.
Imam al-Shafi'i's evidence: Sometimes Imam al-Shafi'i says by way of concession that although action is not exactly command, it is like command in conveying benefit. The evidence is that on the Day of the Trench, when four prayers were missed by the Prophet, when he made them up, he said: 'Pray as you see me praying.'
His statement: 'Obligation is derived from his saying: Pray as you see me praying.'
Our response to the Shafi'is: If the Prophet's action indicated obligation, then he would not need to say 'Pray as you see me praying'. Rather, the Companions would have followed his action simply by seeing it. Thus it is known that obligation is derived from the saying (command), not from the action.
Another evidence of Imam al-Shafi'i: Sometimes Imam al-Shafi'i says by way of advancement that action is a type of command. The evidence is that in the verse 'And the command of Pharaoh is not right' (19:59), the word 'command' is applied to action, because 'right' is a description of action, not of speech. Rather, the description of speech is 'sound'.
His statement: 'He called the action by (the name of) command because it is its cause.'
Our response to the Shafi'is: Command is the cause, and action is the effect. This is naming the cause by the name of the effect. In short, by mentioning 'cause' the 'effect' is intended, while we speak of reality and you have argued using metaphor, which is not acceptable.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a key dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafis and the Shafi'is regarding the indication (dalalah) of the Prophet's actions (fi'l al-nabi) for obligation (wujub). The Hanafi position, as established by Imam Abu Hanifa and his disciples, is that the Prophet's actions in themselves do not indicate obligation. Obligation is only known through a clear verbal command (amr). The evidence for this includes the incident of continuous fasting (wisal) where the Prophet forbade the Companions from imitating him, and the incident of removing sandals in prayer where he explained his action was for a specific reason. Both cases show that the Companions' following of his action was not obligatory. The Shafi'i position, represented by Imam al-Shafi'i, argues that the Prophet's actions do indicate obligation, as evidenced by the hadith 'Pray as you see me praying' where the Prophet commands to follow his action. The Hanafis respond that the obligation in that hadith comes from the verbal command (صلوا), not from the action itself. Another Shafi'i argument is that action is called 'amr' in the Quranic verse 'What is the command of Pharaoh?' (19:59). The Hanafis refute this as metaphorical usage (tasmiat al-sabab bi ism al-musabbab). Thus, the Hanafi school maintains that mere action without a command does not impose obligation.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l): The action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as a source of legal ruling.
امر (amr): A verbal command that indicates obligation.
وجوب (wujub): Legal obligation; the classification of an act as required (fard/wajib).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the primary indicator of obligation (wujub)?
A) The Prophet's action (fi'l) alone.
B) A verbal command (amr).
C) Both action and command equally.
D) Consensus (ijma).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the Prophet's action (fi'l) does not indicate obligation; only a verbal command (amr) does, as demonstrated by the incidents of continuous fasting and sandals removal.

Does the Prophet's Action Imply Obligation? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi critique of Shafi'i proof that the Prophet's action constitutes a command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument against the Shafi'i claim that the Prophet's action constitutes a command, and evaluate the use of metaphor (majaz) in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The Prophet's action alone does not indicate obligation; a verbal command is required for wujub.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: does the Prophet's action (fi'l) itself serve as a command (amr) that imposes obligation? The Shafi'i school argues yes, citing the hadith 'Pray as you see me praying' and a Quranic verse. The Hanafi school rejects this, insisting that obligation must come from an explicit verbal command. Understanding this disagreement clarifies foundational principles of istidlal (legal derivation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed claim
Recognize that the Shafi'i argument claims the Prophet's action (fi'l) itself constitutes a command (amr) that creates obligation (wujub).
The dispute is not about the action being followed, but whether it alone suffices for obligation.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from the hadith
The Hanafi uses the hadith 'صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي' to argue: if the action already implied obligation, the verbal command 'صلوا' would be redundant. Thus, obligation derives from the qawl, not the fi'l.
This argument highlights the Hanafi emphasis on explicit textual commands over inferred implications.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Quranic proof
The Shafi'i cites 'وما أمر فرعون برشيد' (11:97), where 'amr' is applied to action (Pharaoh's command), arguing that action can be called 'amr'.
The term 'رشيد' (right) describes the action, not the speech, supporting that 'amr' here refers to action.
4
Understand the Hanafi response: metaphor (majaz)
The Hanafi responds that this is metaphorical: 'amr' (the cause) is used for the action (the effect) via tasmīyat al-sabab bi-ism al-musabbab. Metaphor cannot establish a legal rule, which requires haqiqah (literal usage).
Hanafi usul strictly separates literal and metaphorical; only literal proofs yield certainty.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute affects how we derive obligation from the Prophet's sunnah. Hanafis require an explicit command or strong contextual evidence; Shafi'is accept the action alone as sufficient in some contexts.
This difference appears in rulings like the obligation of certain prayer acts based on the Prophet's practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اے یعنی نماز اسی طرز پر ادا کر جس طرح تم مجھے دیکھ رہے ہو ۔ اس میں آپ نے اپنی امت پر اپنے افعال کی اتباع کو واجب قرار دیا ہے۔ قوله : والوجوب استفيد بقوله عليه السلام صلوا كمار أيتموني اصلي شوافع کو ہمارا جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ اگر آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا فعل وجوب کا فائدہ دیتا تو پھر آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کو صلو اكمار أيتموني ..... الخ کہنے کی ضرورت پیش نہ آتی کو بلکہ صحابہ آپ کے فعل کو دیکھ کرہی اس پر عمل پیرا ہو جاتے ۔ معلوم ہوا کہ وجوب کا فائدہ صلوا كما ...... الخ یعنی قول (امر) سے حاصل ہوا ہے نہ کہ فعل ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل دیگر کبھی امام شافعی علی سبیل اترقی کہتے ہیں کہ فعل بھی امر کی ایک قسم ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ و ما امر فرعون بر شید میں فعل پر امر کا اطلاق ہوا ہے کیونکہ رشید فعل کی صفت بنتی ہے قول کی صفت نہیں بنتی۔ بلکہ قول کی صفت سدید ہوا کرتی ہے۔ قوله : سمّى الفعل به لأنه سببه شوافع کو ہمارا جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ امر سبب ہے اور فعل مسبب ۔ اور یہ تسمیۃ السبب باسم المسبب کی قبیل سے ہے۔ الغرض یہاں سبب بول کر مسبب مراد لیا ہے جب کہ ہم حقیقت کی بات کرتے ہیں اور آپ نے مجاز سے استدلال کیا ہے جو قابل قبول نہیں۔
O, meaning: Pray in the manner you see me praying. In this, he made following his actions obligatory upon his ummah. His statement: And obligation is derived from his saying, peace be upon him: 'Pray as you see me praying.' Our response to the Shafi'is is: If the Prophet's action had given the benefit of obligation, then there would have been no need for him to say 'Pray as you see me praying'; rather, the Companions would have followed his action merely by seeing it. So it is known that the benefit of obligation is obtained from the saying (command) 'Pray as...' not from the action. Another proof of Imam Shafi'i: Sometimes Imam Shafi'i says, by way of development, that action is also a kind of command; the proof is that in 'And the command of Pharaoh is not right' (Quran 11:97), the term 'command' is applied to action because 'right' is an adjective of action, not of speech; rather, the adjective of speech is 'sound'. His statement: He called the action by it because it is its cause. Our response to the Shafi'is is: The command is the cause and the action is the effect. And this is of the category of calling the cause by the name of the effect. In short, here by mentioning the cause the effect is intended, whereas we are speaking of reality, and you have argued by metaphor, which is not acceptable.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi refutation of the Shafi'i argument that the Prophet's action (fi'l) itself implies obligation. The Hanafi commentator begins by analyzing the hadith 'صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي' (Pray as you see me praying). He argues that if the Prophet's action alone indicated obligation, then the verbal command 'صلوا' would be redundant; the Companions could simply have imitated his action. Therefore, the obligation (wujub) is derived from the explicit command (qawl/amr), not the action. The Shafi'i claim that action is a kind of command is then addressed: they cite the Quranic verse 'وَمَا أَمْرُ فِرْعَوْنَ بِرَشِيدٍ' (And the command of Pharaoh is not right) where 'amr' is applied to action. The Hanafi responds that this is a metaphorical usage (tasmīyat al-sabab bi-ism al-musabbab): the command is the cause and the action is the effect, so calling the action 'amr' is permissible only figuratively. The Hanafi position insists on literal interpretation in legal reasoning, rejecting metaphorical arguments as insufficient for establishing obligation. This dispute illustrates a key methodological difference: Hanafis prioritize explicit verbal commands over deduced implications from actions.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l): The action of the Prophet (or any action) considered as a potential source of legal obligation.
أمر (amr): A command; the linguistic form that demands an action from a higher authority. In this context, it is argued whether it applies only to speech or also to action.
تسمية السبب باسم المسبب (tasmīyat al-sabab bi-ism al-musabbab): A rhetorical figure where the cause is named after the effect. Example: 'amr' (cause) used for the action (effect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the verbal command 'صلوا' would be redundant if action sufficed.
C) Because actions can only indicate permission (ibaha).
D) Because the hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that obligation (wujub) is derived from explicit verbal commands (amr qawli), not from the Prophet's actions alone, rejecting metaphorical extensions of 'amr' as insufficient for legal proof.

Does the Imperative Require Repetition? Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Debate on whether amr implies takrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on repetition (takrar) in amr and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the distinction between mojib and muhtamil.

Core Idea
The imperative (amr) does not inherently require repetition; it is fulfilled by a single act per occurrence.

The debate on whether a command must be repeated when its condition recurs is fundamental in usul al-fiqh. This passage presents the Hanafi position that amr does not imply takrar, and addresses the Shafi'i argument that conditional or descriptive commands do imply repetition. Understanding this distinction is essential for deriving rulings correctly, especially when a Quranic verse or hadith uses a command attached to a condition or description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three groups: (1) those who say amr necessitates repetition (citing the hadith of Akra' ibn Habis), (2) those who say amr only potentially implies repetition (based on the indefinite noun in the imperative), and (3) the Shafi'i position that conditional/descriptive commands imply repetition. Identify each group's claim and evidence.
The Hanafi position is the last one stated: amr does not carry the possibility of repetition, even when conditional.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'is argue from Quranic verses: 'If you are junub, purify yourselves' and 'The thief, cut off their hands.' They claim these commands must be repeated each time the condition (janabah) or description (theft) occurs. Examine the verses and understand how this leads to the claim of takrar.
Note that the Shafi'i position is attributed to 'some Shafi'is,' not the entire school.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that when the condition or description is found, the ruling applies anew each time. This is not because the command itself implies repetition, but because the cause (sabab) is renewed. The command text remains the same; each separate occurrence is a separate application of the same command.
This is a key distinction: the renewal of the cause, not the inherent nature of the command, produces multiple acts.
4
Apply the mojib vs. muhtamil distinction
The passage introduces 'mojib' (necessitator) as something established without additional evidence, and 'muhtamil' (possible) as requiring external indication. Understand that the Hanafi view treats amr as mojib for one performance, while the Shafi'i view treats it as muhtamil for repetition when conditions exist.
This distinction is a methodological tool in Hanafi usul to classify texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خطۃ فرمائیے: جو لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ تکرار کا تقاضہ کرتا ہے ان کی دلیل اقرع بن حابس رضی اللہ تعالی عنہ کی حدیث ہے۔ کہ وہ اہل لسان میں سے تھے اس کے باوجود انہوں نے حج کے حکم سے تکرار سمجھ لیا۔ جو لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ امر تکرار کا احتمال رکھتا ہے ان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ صیغہ امر مثلا اضرب ہے اطلب منك ضرباً کا اور ضربا نکرہ ہے اور نکرہ اگر چہ اثبات میں خاص ہوتا ہے اور عموم کا احتمال رکھتا ہے لہذا جب کوئی قرینہ ہو گا تو اس کو عموم پر عمل کیا جائیگا۔ دونوں جماعتوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق موجب اور محتمل میں فرق یہ ہے کہ موجب بغیر نسیت کے ثابت ہو جاتا ہے اور محتمل نیست کے ساتھ ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ شوافع کا تیر اند ہے بعض شوافع کا خیال ہے کہ اگر امر معلق بشرط ہو یا مخصوص بوصف ہو تو وہ تکرار کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ شوافع کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالی نے فرمایا (وان كنتم جنباً فاطهروا ) کہ جب جنابت کی شرط پائی جائیگی تو طہارت کا حکم پایا جائیگا اور السارق والسارقة فا قطعوا أيد يهما ... الخ ) جب سرقہ کا وصف پایا جائے گا تو قطع ید کاحکم بھی پایا جائے گا ۔ ظاہر احتمال لیکن ہمارے نزدیک امر خواه معلق بشرط ہو یا مخصوص بالوصف ہو بہر صورت امر تکرار کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا۔ شوافع کی دلیل کا جواب ہم کہتے ہیں کہ جب بھی جنابت اور سرقہ کا وصف پایا جائے گا تو طہارت اور فاقطعوا کا حکم الگ متوجہ ہوگا ۔
He says: Those who say that (the command) requires repetition, their proof is the hadith of Akra' ibn Habis (may Allah be pleased with him) that he was from the people of eloquence, yet he understood repetition from the command of Hajj. Those who say that the command carries the possibility of repetition, their proof is that the imperative form, for example 'idrib' (strike), is a request for striking from you, and 'darban' (a striking) is an indefinite noun. And an indefinite noun, although in affirmation it is specific, carries the possibility of generality. So when there is a contextual indicator, it will be acted upon as general. The difference between the two groups: the difference between 'mojib' (necessitator) and 'muhtamil' (possible) is that the necessitator is established without any indication, while the possible is established with an indication. The Shafi'is' view: Some Shafi'is hold that if the command is conditional on a condition or specified by an attribute, then it carries the possibility of repetition. Their proof is that Allah Almighty said: 'And if you are in a state of janabah, then purify yourselves' — when the condition of janabah is found, the ruling of purification applies; and 'the thief, male and female, cut off their hands' — when the description of theft is found, the ruling of amputation applies. That is apparent possibility. But in our view, whether the command is conditional on a condition or specified by an attribute, in any case the command does not carry the possibility of repetition. Response to the Shafi'is' proof: We say that whenever the condition of janabah and the description of theft are found, the rulings of 'purify' and 'cut off' apply separately each time.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule discussion on whether the imperative form (amr) requires repetition (takrar) or merely one occurrence. The author presents three positions: (1) Some argue that amr necessitates repetition, citing the hadith of Akra' ibn Habis who understood Hajj must be repeated annually. (2) Others argue that amr only potentially implies repetition, because the imperative is a request for an action expressed as an indefinite noun (nakirah), which in affirmative contexts is specific but can carry generality if a contextual indicator (qarinah) is present. (3) The Shafi'is add that when the command is conditional (e.g., 'if you are junub, purify') or descriptive (e.g., 'the thief, cut off'), it implies repetition each time the condition or description occurs. The author states the Hanafi position: even in such cases, the command does not imply repetition; it applies separately each time the condition is met, but that is not due to the command itself requiring repetition. The distinction between 'mojib' (necessitator) and 'muhtamil' (possible) is that a mojib gives certainty without need for additional indication, while muhtamil requires external evidence. The Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof is that when the condition or description is found, the ruling applies anew each time; this is not because the command text itself necessitates repetition but because the condition is renewed. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that amr, by itself, only demands one performance of the minimal act.

Key Terms
امر (amr): The imperative form used to demand an action, e.g., 'idrib' (strike).
تکرار (takrar): Repetition of the commanded act multiple times.
موجب (mojib): A text or proof that necessitates a ruling without need for additional evidence.
محتمل (muhtamil): A text or proof that carries possibility but requires external indication to become operative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It does not require repetition and is fulfilled by one performance.
C) It only implies repetition if attached to a condition or description.
D) It necessitates the minimum act but also implies repetition potentially.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative form does not inherently require repetition; each occurrence of a condition or description triggers a fresh application of the command.
