8 min
Does the Imperative Imply Repetition? Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Argues that the imperative does not imply repetition, addressing objections and comparing with Shafi'i view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the imperative does not imply repetition to a divorce scenario and evaluate the Shafi'i counterposition.

Core Idea
The imperative does not imply repetition; repetition comes from recurring causes, not the command itself.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul dispute: does the imperative (amr) form demand repetition (takrar) every time it is issued? The Hanafi school argues it does not, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds it can. The Hanafi position has practical consequences in divorce, worship obligations, and beyond. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the central question of this dispute?
Is the imperative a one-time command or does it imply multiple performances?
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds the imperative applies to the least of its genus but can imply all; it does not inherently demand repetition.
Key terms: aqall jins, takrar.
3
Examine the divorce application
In 'talliqi nafsik', the imperative falls on one divorce, unless one intends three (the whole genus). Intending two is invalid except for a slave woman.
Why is two invalid? Because it is neither the least nor the whole genus.
4
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
The imperative is derived from the infinitive, which is singular and does not denote number. Therefore, the command itself does not imply multiplicity.
Compare with active participle (ism fa'il) which also indicates the infinitive without number.
5
Address the objection from worship repetition
Worship like prayer repeats, but that is due to recurring causes (time), not the original command. New causes generate new commands.
Hajj is obligatory once because its cause (the House) is single.
6
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the imperative can imply repetition because it is a general command that continues until fulfilled.
Consequence: husband's intention of two divorces in 'talliqi nafsik' is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لكنه يقع على اقل جنسه ويحتمل كله حتى اذا قال لها طلقى نفسك انه يقع على الواحد الا أن ينوى الثلث . جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ طلاق کا اقل جنس یعنی فرد حقیقی تو ایک ہے لیکن اس کا فرد کی اور کل جنس تین ہے۔ تو امر کا اطلاق تو اقل جنس پر ہوتا ہے لیکن کل جنس کا بھی احتمال رکھتا ہے تو تین کی نیت کرنا صیح ہو گا لیکن دو کی نیت کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ دو نہ تو فر حقیقی ہے اور نہ حکمی ہے ہاں اگر عورت لونڈی ہو تو پھر دوکی نیت کرنا صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ لونڈی کے حق میں دو کل جنس ہے اور فرد چکی ہے۔ جواب پراعتراض... اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب طلاق دو کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا تو پھر طلقی نفسک ثنتین کہتا کس طرح صحیح ہو گیا۔ یعنی ثنتین طلاق کے لئے بیان تفسیر کس طرح بن گیا۔ جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ ثنتین طلقی نفسک کے لئے بیان تغیر نہیں ہے بلکہ اپنے ماقبل کے لئے بیان تغییر ہے۔
قوله : لأن صيغة الأمر مختصرة من طلب الفعل بالمصدر الذي هو فرد ومعنى التوحد مرعي في الفاظ الوحدان وذلك بالفردية والجنسية والمثنى بمعزل عنهما .
عدم تکرار پر احناف کی دلیل
احناف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ امر کا صیغہ یہ مختصر ہے طلب فعل بالمصدر سے مثلا ( اضرب ) نتقرب الطلب منك الضرب كا اور مصدر کا لفظ تو مفرد ہے اور مفرد الفاظ میں مفرد معنی کی رعایت کی جاتی ہے؟ لہذا لفظ مفرد تکرار کا تقاضہ نہیں کرتا تو جو اس سے مختصر ہے یعنی امر وہ بھی تکرار کا تقاضہ نہیں کریگا
اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ جب امر تکرار کا تقاضہ نہیں کرتا تو عبادات کیوں منکر رہوتی ہیں ۔ حالانکہ عبادات کا وجوب بھی باری تعالٰی عزوجل کے امر کی بناء پر ہے۔ قوله : وما تكرر من العبادات فبأسبابها لا بالا وامر
جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ عبادات امر کی وجہ سے حکمر نہیں ہوتیں بلکہ اسباب کی وجہ سے مشکر ر ہوتی ہے ملالہ وقت یہ وجوب صلوۃ کا سبب ہے اور مضان روزوں کے واجب ہونے کا سبب ہے تو جب بھی وقت اور رمضان متشکر رہو گے تو نماز اور روزے بھی منکر رہوں گے۔ یہی وجہ ہے حج سال میں ایک مرتبہ فرض ہوتا ہے کیونکہ اس کا سبب بیت اللہ شریف ہے اور وہ ایک ہی ہے اگر تکرار امر کی وجہ سے ہوتا تو حج میں بھی تکرار ہونا چاہئے تھا۔ اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ یہ بھی تو ہو سکتا ہے کہ وقت نفس وجوب کا سبب ہو اور امر وجوب اداء کا سبب ہو ۔
جواب .... جواب یہ ہے کہ جس وقت سبب پایا جائیگا اس وقت اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف سے بندہ نائی کی طرف کی جانب نیا امر متوجہ ہو گا۔ مثلاً جب بھی نماز کا وقت آیگا تو اللہ تعالی کی طرف سے صلوۃ کا نیا حکم آئیگا ۔ اس لئے وجوب بھی نئے سرے سے آئے گا علی ھذا القیاس۔
قوله : وعند الشافعى لما احتمل التكرار تملك ان تطلق نفسها ثنتين اذانوى الزوج
امام شافعی کے نزدیک چونکہ امر تکرار کا احتمال رکھتا ہے لہذا اگر شوہر نے طلقی نفسک میں دو طلاقوں کی نیت کر لی تو بیوی کو اختیار حاصل ہو گا کہ وہ اپنے آپ کو دو طلاقیں دے۔
قوله : وكذا اسم الفاعل يدل على المصدر لغة ولا يحتمل العدد
He said: But it falls on the least of its genus and implies all of it, such that when he says to her 'Divorce yourself,' it falls on one unless he intends three. Answer: The answer is that the least of the genus of divorce, i.e., the true individual, is one, but its individual and the whole genus is three. So the imperative applies to the least genus but also implies the whole genus, so intending three is valid but intending two is not valid because two is neither a true individual nor a legal individual. However, if the woman is a slave girl, then intending two is valid because for a slave girl, two is the whole genus and the individual. Objection to the answer: The objection is that if divorce does not imply two, then why is saying 'Divorce yourself two' valid? That is, how did 'two' become an explanatory statement for divorce? The answer is that 'two' is not a statement of change for 'divorce yourself' but rather a statement of change for what precedes it.
He said: Because the imperative form is derived from the request of the action using the infinitive, which is singular, and the meaning of oneness is observed in singular words, through individuality and generality, and the dual is separate from both.
The Hanafi evidence for non-repetition: The Hanafi evidence is that the imperative form is derived from the request of the action using the infinitive, e.g., 'Strike' is derived from 'I request from you striking', and the infinitive word is singular, and singular words observe singular meaning? Hence, the singular word does not require repetition, so what is derived from it, i.e., the imperative, will also not require repetition.
Objection: The objection is that if the imperative does not require repetition, then why are acts of worship repeated? Whereas the obligation of acts of worship is based on the command of Allah Almighty. He said: What is repeated among acts of worship is due to their causes, not due to the original command.
Answer: The answer is that acts of worship are not repeated due to the command but are repeated due to causes. For example, time is the cause for the obligation of prayer, and Ramadan is the cause for the obligation of fasting. So whenever time and Ramadan occur, prayer and fasting will be repeated. This is why Hajj is obligatory once a year, because its cause is the noble House of Allah, and it is one. If repetition were due to the command, then Hajj would have to be repeated. Objection: The objection is that it is also possible that time is the cause of the obligation itself and the command is the cause of the obligation of performance.
Answer: The answer is that whenever the cause is found, a new command will be directed from Allah to the servant. For example, whenever the time for prayer comes, a new command for prayer comes from Allah. Therefore, the obligation will also come anew. And so on analogically.
He said: According to al-Shafi'i, since the imperative implies repetition, she has the right to divorce herself two if the husband intended it.
According to Imam Shafi'i, since the imperative implies repetition, if the husband intends two divorces in 'Divorce yourself,' the wife has the right to give herself two divorces.
He said: Similarly, the active participle indicates the infinitive linguistically and does not imply number.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of the imperative (amr) and whether it implies repetition. The author begins by discussing the meaning of 'talliqi nafsik' (divorce yourself). The Hanafi position is that the imperative, when unrestricted, applies to the least of its genus (one divorce) but can also imply the whole genus (three divorces) if one intends it. Thus, intending three is valid, but intending two is invalid because two is not a true or legal individual. An exception is made for slave women where two is the maximum. The passage then addresses an objection: if the imperative does not imply two, why can one say 'divorce yourself two'? The answer is that 'two' is not an explanatory statement for the imperative but rather changes the meaning of the preceding phrase. Next, the Hanafi evidence is presented: the imperative is derived from the infinitive, which is singular and does not denote number. Therefore, the imperative also does not imply repetition. An objection arises: if that is true, why are acts of worship repeated? The response is that repetition in worship comes from their causes (asbab), such as time and seasons, not from the command itself. For example, prayer is repeated because of the arrival of new times. The Shafi'i view is then given: because the imperative can imply repetition, the husband's intention for two divorces is valid, and the wife may execute them. Finally, it is noted that the active participle (ism fa'il) also indicates the infinitive and does not imply number, reinforcing the Hanafi argument.

Key Terms
امر (amr): The imperative form requesting an action, derived from the infinitive.
تکرار (takrar): Repetition or multiplicity of the commanded act.
اسم فاعل (ism fa'il): The active participle, which linguistically indicates the infinitive and does not imply number.
اقل جنس (aqall jins): The least of the genus; the minimum individual representation of a category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the reason for the repetition of acts of worship (e.g., prayer)?
A) The imperative command itself demands repetition.
B) The repetition is due to the recurrence of causes such as time.
C) The infinitive from which the imperative is derived implies multiplicity.
D) The active participle indicates number.

Key Takeaway
The imperative does not imply repetition; repetition is caused by recurring factors other than the command itself.

Linguistic Analysis in Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Repeated Theft
Hanafi response to Shafi'i argument on repeated theft using verse and hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key linguistic arguments (ism al-fā'il and command form) in the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i hadith on repeated theft, and apply the Hanafi rule that the active participle indicates a single act.

Core Idea
A thief's hand is cut only once per theft; the Shafi'i reading of the hadith is overridden by linguistic analysis and istihsan.

This lesson examines the classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the punishment for repeated theft. While both schools agree on cutting the right hand for the first theft and the left foot for the second, they diverge on the third and fourth thefts. The Hanafi school relies on the Quranic verse's linguistic structure to argue that only one limb can be cut per theft, whereas the Shafi'i school cites a hadith that appears to authorize multiple cuttings. We will follow the Hanafi argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly state what the Hanafi school rules for first, second, third, and subsequent thefts. Note that only one hand is cut per theft, then foot, then imprisonment.
The ruling is based on the verse, consensus, and istihsan.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
State that the Shafi'i school uses a hadith with repeated 'fa-iqta'u' to justify cutting the left hand on third theft.
The hadith is mentioned in the source as having four 'cut off' commands.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
Explain the linguistic analysis: the active participle 'sāriq' and the command 'fa-iqta'u' both indicate a single act, not multiple. This is the core usul argument.
The Hanafi interpret the hadith as applying to the first theft only, or as a different context.
4
Follow the objections and answers
Two objections are raised: 1) Why cut foot on second theft if only one cut per theft? Answer: foot cutting comes from consensus, not the verse. 2) Why repeat lashes in zina? Answer: skin can be lashed repeatedly, but a cut limb cannot be cut again.
The second objection uses a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) rebutted by istihsan.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Shafi'i cuts left hand on third theft; Hanafi imprisons instead. This affects actual hudud implementation.
The author supports the Hanafi position as 'our near view'.

Source Text

قوله حتى لا يراد بآية السرقة السرقة واحدة وبالفعل الواحد لا تقطع الأيدي واحدة اس بات پر کہ اسم فاعل بھی تعداد اور تکرار کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا مصنف نے یہ تفریع اٹھائی ہے کہ آیت سرقہ میں سرقه السارق والسارقة فاقطعوا سے صرف ایک ہی سرقہ مراد ہوگا ۔ اس میں امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ چور تیسری اور چوتھی مرتبہ چوری کرے تو اس کا بایاں ہاتھ اور دایاں پاؤں کاٹ دیا جائے گا ان کی دلیل حدیث ہے۔ حضور اکرم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا: جو چوری کرے تو کاٹ دو پھر چوری کرے تو کاٹ دو پھر چوری کرے تو کاٹ ڈالو پھر چوری کرے تو کاٹ ڈالو ( حدیث عربی متن کے ساتھ کتاب کے حاشیہ میں مذکور ہے جس میں چار مرتبہ فاقطعوا کے الفاظ ہیں) جب کہ ہمارے نزدیک تیسری دفعہ چوری کرنے کی وجہ سے اس کا بایاں ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائیگا۔ بلکہ اسے جیل میں ڈال دیا جائیگا یہاں تک کہ توبہ کرلے یا مر جائے۔ احناف کی دلیل ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ سارق اسم فاعل ہے جو مصدری معنی پر لغتہ دلالت کرتا ہے لہذا اس سے یا تو فرد حقیقی مراد ہوگا یا فرد کمی اور سرقہ کا فرد حکمی تو معلوم نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ اس کا مدار آخر عمر پر ہے ۔ لہذا ایک سرقہ سے ایک دفعہ ہاتھ کاٹنا مراد ہوگا۔ اس طرح فاقطعوا جو کہ امر ہے یہ بھی ایک ہی قطع پر دلالت کرتا ہے۔ اعتراض اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ پھر آپ دوسری دفعہ بایاں پاؤں کیوں کا نتے ہیں۔ حاصل یہ ہے کہ جب آپ کہتے ہیں کہ ایک سرقہ سے ایک ہی دفعہ قطع ید کا حکم لاگو ہوگا تو ہونا یہ چاہئے کہ جب چور دوسری مرتبہ چوری کرے تو اس کا بایاں پیر نہ کاٹا جائے۔ جواب جواب یہ ہے کہ آیت میں پاؤں کا کوئی ذکر نہیں لہذا پاؤں کٹنے کو ہم اس امر سے نہیں بلکہ دوسری نص سے ثابت کر سکتے ہیں۔ امی نے کہا ہے کہ ید یمنی کا قطع نص سے اور رجل یرنی کا اجماع ۔ ثابت ہے ۔ اعتراض پھر اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ زنا کی صورت میں غیر محصن زانی کو بار بار کیوں کوڑے لگاتے ہیں یہاں بھی فاجلدوا امر ہے جس سے ایک ہی مرتبہ جلد ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ جواب ..... جواب یہ ہے کہ کوڑے جلد پر لگتے ہیں اور جلد بار بار کوڑوں کا محل بن سکتی ہے بخلاف قطع ید کے کہ اس کا ہاتھ ایک دفعہ کٹنے کے بعد دوبارہ قطع کا محل نہیں بن سکتا۔ لہذا دوسری مرتبہ پیر کاٹا جائے گا۔
His statement: 'so that the verse of theft does not intend one theft, and by one act the hands are not cut one time.' On this point, that the active participle also does not indicate number and repetition, the author has derived that in the verse of theft, 'the male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands' means only one theft is intended. Regarding this, Imam al-Shafi'i says that if a thief steals a third and a fourth time, his left hand and right foot should be cut. His evidence is a hadith: The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever steals, cut off; then if he steals, cut off; then if he steals, cut off; then if he steals, cut off' (the hadith is mentioned in the margin of the book with its Arabic text, containing four times the word 'fa-iqta'u'). Whereas according to us, on the third theft his left hand is not cut; rather he is imprisoned until he repents or dies. The evidence of the Hanafis: our evidence is that 'sāriq' is an active participle that indicates a verbal meaning linguistically; therefore it intends either a real individual or a notional individual, and the notional individual of theft cannot be known because it depends on the end of life. So from one theft, one hand-cutting is intended. Similarly, 'fa-iqta'u' which is a command also indicates a single cutting. Objection: Then why do you cut the left foot the second time? The point: when you say that one theft entails one hand-cutting, it should follow that when the thief steals a second time, his left foot should not be cut. Answer: The verse does not mention the foot; therefore we do not prove foot cutting from this command but from another text. The Imam said: cutting of the right hand is from the text, and cutting of the left foot is from consensus. Objection: Then in the case of fornication, why for the non-muhsan adulterer are lashes repeated? Here also 'fa-jlidū' is a command that implies a single lashing. Answer: Lashes strike the skin, and the skin can be the locus for repeated lashes, unlike hand-cutting, where the hand after being cut once is not a locus for another cutting. Therefore, the second time the foot is cut.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute regarding the punishment for repeated theft. The author first states the matn point that the verse of theft intends only one theft, not multiple. He then contrasts the Shafi'i position, which uses a hadith to justify cutting the left hand and right foot on the third and fourth theft, with the Hanafi position that only one hand is cut per theft and subsequent thefts lead to imprisonment. The Hanafi evidence is based on the linguistic analysis of the active participle 'sāriq' and the command 'fa-iqta'u', which both indicate a single act. An objection is raised: if only one cut per theft, why cut the foot the second time? The answer is that foot cutting is proven by another text and consensus, not from the same verse. Another objection compares zina lashes, where repeated lashing is allowed because the skin can be lashed again; the Hanafi answer distinguishes by noting that a cut limb cannot be cut again, hence foot cutting is a separate punishment. The argument uses istihsan (juristic preference) based on the nature of the body part.

Key Terms
سارق (sāriq): Active participle from saraqa, indicating the doer of the act of theft; linguistically implies a verbal meaning (masdar) and thus a single theft.
قطع (qaṭ'): Cutting off the hand as a hudud punishment for theft; in this context, it is a single act per theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) His left hand is cut off.
B) His left foot is cut off.
C) He is imprisoned until repentance or death.
D) He is given lashes.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts the Quranic penalty to one cutting per theft based on the linguistic form of the active participle and command, and supplements with other evidence for subsequent punishments.

Qada and Its Cause: Hanafi Response to Shafi'i Objection on I'tikaf
Hanafi response to Shafi'i objection on qada of i'tikaf nadhr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument that qada may have a different cause than ada, using the example of qada of a vowed i'tikaf in Ramadan.

Core Idea
A missed vow's qada reverts to the original obligation when the concession (rukhsah) is lost.

This passage addresses a usul dispute: does qada necessarily share the same cause as ada? The Shafi'is argue yes, but Hanafis respond with a nuanced view involving concessions and original obligations. We'll dissect the reasoning and see how the Hanafi school uses the concept of rukhsah to distinguish between the cause of performance and the cause of makeup.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its underlying usul assumption.
First, read the Shafi'i objection carefully. What is the specific scenario they present? What assumption do they make about the cause of qada?
The Shafi'is assume that if qada is due to the same cause as ada, then qada in another Ramadan should be valid.
2
State the Hanafi position on qada of i'tikaf nadhr.
What is the Hanafi ruling? Under what conditions does the Hanafi school allow or disallow qada in another Ramadan?
Hanafis say qada is not performed in another Ramadan but in a separate month of fasts.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: the role of rukhsah.
How does the Hanafi response explain the shift from the original rule (separate fasts) to the concession (using Ramadan fasts)? What happens when the missed i'tikaf is not performed?
The link to Ramadan was a concession based on virtue; when the vow is missed, the ruling returns to the original.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
What is the practical consequence of this dispute? How does the ruling differ between the two schools?
A Hanafi must perform qada in a separate month; a Shafi'i can do it in any month, including another Ramadan.
5
Consider the implications for the usul principle of causality.
What does this dispute teach us about the Hanafi approach to causality in obligations? How do concessions affect the cause of qada?
Hanafis are flexible: the cause can revert to the original when a concession is no longer applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفيما اذا نذر أن يعتكف شهر رمضان فصام ولم يعتكف انما وجب القضاء بصوم مقصود لعود شرطه الى الكمال لا لأن القضاء وجب بسبب بآخر
ایک مشہور اعتراض : احناف پر شوافع کی طرف سے ایک مشہور سوال ہوتا ہے اور وہ یہ کہ اگر کسی نے نذر مانی کہ میں رمضان کے مہینے کا اعتکاف کرونگا پھر اس نے روزے تو رکھ لئے لیکن کسی عذر کی وجہ سے اعتکاف نہ کر سکا تو احناف کے نزدیک وہ اس اعتکاف کی قضاء کسی دوسرے رمضان میں نہیں کر دیگا بلکہ وہ مستقل ایک مہینے کے نفلی روزے رکھ کر ان میں اعتکاف کی قضاء کریگا۔ شوافع استدلال کرتے ہیں کہ آپ کے مذہب کے مطابق اگر قضا اسی سبب سے واجب ہوتی جس سے ادا واجب ہوئی تھی تو دوسرے رمضان میں اس اعتکاف کی قضاء صحیح ہونی چاہئے حالانکہ احناف کے نزدیک یہ قضائے اعتکاف صحیح نہیں ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ قضاء کا سبب ادا کا سبب نہیں ہے۔
جواب ۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اعتکاف روزوں کے بغیر صحیح نہیں ہوتا جب کوئی اعتکاف کی نذر مانے تو روزے بھی واجب ہو جاتے ہیں تو اصل تو یہ تھی کہ یہ شخص ایک مہینے کے الگ روزے رکھتا اور ان میں اپنی نذر کو پوری کرتا مگر رمضان کی فضیلت کی بناء پر ہم نے اس اصل کو چھوڑ دیا اور ہم نے اجازت دیدی کہ وہ رمضان کے روزوں میں ہی اپنی نذر پوری کرے۔ مگر جب رمضان کی فضیلت کو حاصل نہ کر سکا تو حکم اپنی اصل کی طرف لوٹ گیا یعنی یہ کہ وہ مستقل روزے رکھ کر اعتکاف کی قضاء کرے اب اگر دوسرا رمضان آ بھی گیا تو حکم اس اصل سے رمضان ثانی کی طرف منتقل نہ ہو گا ویسے بھی رمضان ثانی تک اسکا زندہ رہنا امر موہوم ہے۔
His saying: And regarding if one vowed to perform i'tikaf in the month of Ramadan, then he fasted but did not perform i'tikaf, then qada becomes obligatory with a specific fast because its condition returns to completeness, not because the qada became obligatory due to another cause. A famous objection: The Shafi'is raise a famous question against the Hanafis: If someone vowed to perform i'tikaf in the month of Ramadan, then he kept the fasts but due to an excuse could not perform i'tikaf, then according to the Hanafis, he will not make qada of this i'tikaf in another Ramadan; rather, he will keep a separate month of supererogatory fasts and in them make qada of i'tikaf. The Shafi'is argue that according to your madhhab, if qada becomes obligatory due to the same cause from which the original performance became obligatory, then making qada of this i'tikaf in another Ramadan should be valid, whereas according to the Hanafis this qada of i'tikaf is not valid. So it is known that the cause of qada is not the same as the cause of ada. Response: The answer is that i'tikaf is not valid without fasting. When someone vows i'tikaf, fasting also becomes obligatory. So the original rule was that this person would keep a separate month of fasts and fulfil his vow in them, but due to the virtue of Ramadan we abandoned this original rule and allowed him to fulfil his vow in the fasts of Ramadan. But when he could not attain the virtue of Ramadan, the ruling returned to its original, i.e., he should keep separate fasts and make qada of i'tikaf. Now if another Ramadan comes, the ruling will not transfer from this original to the second Ramadan; besides, his remaining alive until the second Ramadan is a remote possibility.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the cause of qada. The Shafi'is object: According to the Hanafi principle that qada is due to the same cause as ada (performance), if someone vows i'tikaf in Ramadan but misses it, the qada should be performable in another Ramadan (since the cause, the vow, remains). However, Hanafis say qada is not valid in another Ramadan; rather, one must keep a separate month of fasts and perform i'tikaf then. The Hanafi response clarifies that i'tikaf requires fasting; the vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan originally obligated both the fasts and the i'tikaf. By the virtue of Ramadan, we allowed the Ramadan fasts to serve both the obligation of the month and the vow, but when the vow is missed, it reverts to the original rule: separate fasts. The second Ramadan does not automatically revive the connection because the original obligation was not tied to Ramadan per se but to the vow; the link to Ramadan was only a concession (rukhsah) based on virtue. Thus, the cause of qada is not the same as the cause of ada; it is the original obligation that returns. This shows that qada can have a different cause than ada, contrary to the Shafi'i assumption.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up for an obligatory act that was missed; the re-performance of an act after its time or in lieu of a missed obligation.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, making it obligatory.
اعتکاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in a mosque with the intention of worship, typically requiring fasting.
رخصت (rukhsah): A concession granted due to a special circumstance, such as the virtue of Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why is qada of a missed i'tikaf vow not valid in another Ramadan?
A) Because i'tikaf is not valid in Ramadan without a specific intention.
B) Because the original obligation required separate fasts, and the link to Ramadan was only a concession.
C) Because qada must be in a month with no fasting.
D) Because the vow becomes invalid after missing it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, qada may have a different cause than ada when a concession (rukhsah) is lost, reverting to the original obligation.

Defending Qada of a Missed Sacrifice by Charity Against the Objection of Unintelligibility
Defending the permissibility of compensating a missed sacrifice with its value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection regarding qada of a ghayr ma'qul act and evaluate the Hanafi response allowing compensation by charity.

Core Idea
A ghayr ma'qul act can still be made up by a rationally equivalent alternative like charity.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if an act of worship is not rationally understood (ghayr ma'qul), how can it be made up? The Hanafi school responds that even such acts can be compensated by their value or a similar act, drawing on the principle that obligations can be fulfilled by alternatives when the original is impossible.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the objector's argument: why does he claim that qada of sacrifice is impermissible? Note that he calls the sacrifice an 'unintelligible matter' (amr ghayr ma'qul) because it involves destroying animals.
The objector's reasoning: if the act is not rationally conceived, then making it up is also not conceivable.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
Find the ruling that the text attributes to the Hanafis: what must a poor person do who vowed a sacrifice and lost the animal? The text says he must give charity of a sheep or its value.
This shows that the Hanafi school permits compensation by value even for a ghayr ma'qul act.
3
Analyse the basis of the objection
Why is sacrifice considered ghayr ma'qul? The objector claims it is irrational because it involves destroying animals, which seems wasteful. Understand that the objector believes the act's wisdom is not comprehensible, so qada should not be possible.
The objector may be from a school that requires acts of worship to be rationally intelligible for substitution.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
How does the ruling on charity address the objection? The Hanafis allow qada by giving the animal itself or its value as charity, not by a literal repetition of the slaughter. This shows that the obligation is not tied to the specific act but can be fulfilled by an alternative means.
The response implies that qada is permissible even if the original act is ghayr ma'qul, as long as the substitute is valid.
5
Connect to usul principles
What usul principle justifies this? The Hanafi ruling may be based on istihsan (juristic preference) or the principle that obligations can be fulfilled by alternatives when the original becomes impossible (similar to dam for missed wajibat in Hajj).
The objector relies on strict analogy (qiyas) requiring the qada to be identical to the original, which the Hanafi school rejects here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كالتصدق بالقيمة عند قوات أيام التضحية اعتراض ... ایک اور اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ قربانی کے دنوں میں قربانی کرنا ایک امر غیر معقول ہے کیونکہ اس میں حیوانوں کو تلف کرنا لازم آتا ہے۔ تو ہونا یہ چاہیئے کہ اس کی قضاء نہ ہو ۔ حالانکہ تم کہتے ہو کہ اگر کسی فقیر نے قربانی کرنے کی نذر مان لی یا کسی شخص نے قربانی کا جانور خر ید لیا پھر وہ ہلاک ہو گیا تو اس فقیر پر واجب ہے کہ وہ عین شاہ صدقہ کرے یا اس کی قیمت کا صدقہ
He said: 'Like charity of value at the time of the days of sacrifice' is an objection. Another objection is: slaughtering during the days of sacrifice is an unintelligible matter because it involves destroying animals. So it should not have a qada. Yet you say that if a poor person made a vow to sacrifice, or someone bought an animal for sacrifice and it died, then that poor person is obligated to give charity of a sheep or its value.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents another objection regarding qada for sacrifice. The objector argues that sacrifice (udhiya) is a form of animal slaughter, which is apparently wasteful and 'unintelligible' (ghayr ma'qul). Therefore, if the sacrifice is missed, it should not be made up by charity. However, the text indicates that the Hanafi position is that if a poor person vows a sacrifice and the animal is lost, he must give the animal itself (if available) or its value as charity. This shows that qada can be performed by a similar act (like giving the animal) or by its value even when the original act is 'unintelligible'. The text seems to be cut off, but the objection and the implicit ruling are clear.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed act of worship, here applied to a missed sacrifice
امر غیر معقول (amr ghayr ma'qul): An act whose rationale is not intelligible to reason, requiring textual basis

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Nothing is required
C) Give charity of the animal or its value

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits qada of a missed sacrifice by giving charity, despite the objection that sacrifice is ghayr ma'qul.

Classification of Restitution: Formal vs Meaningful Like
Classifies restitution and states a key usul principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul and qada' mushabil al-ada', and state the Hanafi usul principle that formal likeness (mithl suri) takes precedence over meaningful likeness (mithl ma'nawi).

Core Idea
Formal likeness in restitution takes precedence over value equivalence; only when formal likeness is impossible does value become the measure.

In Islamic law, compensation for damages often involves a choice between delivering the exact physical equivalent or its monetary value. This lesson introduces the Hanafi classification of restitution and establishes the principle that formal resemblance is the primary criterion, with illustrations from diyah and marriage dower.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qada'
Locate the two categories of restitution mentioned in the text: 'qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul' (restitution with an unusual equivalent) and 'qada' mushabil al-ada'' (restitution resembling fulfillment). Understand that the first applies when no natural monetary measure exists, while the second applies when value substitution is treated as fulfillment.
Focus on the definitions: the first is for life/limbs, the second for fungible like items.
2
Examine the example of diyah
Read the example of accidental killing or cutting of limbs: the criminal must pay diyah (blood money) even though human life and limbs have no understood similarity to property. This is qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul.
The compensation is by property despite no formal likeness — a concession to practicality.
3
Examine the example of marriage dower
Read the case of a man marrying a woman with an unspecified slave as dower: if he gives a medium-quality slave, it is fulfillment (ada'); if he gives its value, it is qada' but considered as ada'. The criterion for ending the dispute is value.
This is qada' mushabil al-ada' — the value payment stands in for the physical item.
4
Extract the principle
From these examples, the text derives the usul rule: 'al-mithl al-suri muqaddam ala al-mithl al-ma'nawi' (the formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like). Understand that formal likeness refers to physical resemblance; meaningful likeness refers to equivalence in value.
This principle governs which type of restitution is preferred in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بالمسمى قضاء مشابه بالا داء کی مثال : قضاء مشابہ بالاداء کی مثال یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی آدمی نے عورت کیساتھ شادی کی غیر معین غلام کو بدلے میں تو اگر شوہر درمیانے درجے کا غلام خرید کر بیوی کو دیدے تو ادا ہوگئی اور اگر اس کی قیمت دے دے تو یہ قضاء ہوگئی لیکن اداء کے معنی میں ہوگی اس لئے کہ میاں بیوی کے درمیان غلام کے بارے میں جو جھگڑا ہو سکتا ہے اس کو ختم کرنے کا معیار قیمت ہے۔ کیونکہ جو اعلیٰ قیمت والا غلام ہو گا وہ اعنی ہوگا اور جو کم قیمت والا ہو گا وہ ارنی ہوگا اور جو درمیانی قیمت والا ہو گا وہ متو ا ہوگا یا قیمت کا دینا بھی ادا کے معنی میں ہے۔ لہذا عورت کو قیمت کے قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ مثل صوری مثل معنوی سے مقدم ہے اس پر امام صاحب دو تفریعیں اٹھاتے ہیں۔ مثل صوری مثل معنوی پر مقدم ہے۔ قوله : وعلى هذا قال ابو حنيفة في القطع ثم القتل عمداً للولى فعلهما (1) پہلی تفریح اگر کسی ظالم نے کسی آدمی کا پہلے ہاتھ کا نا عمدا پھر اس شخص مجروح کو تندرست ہونے سے پہلے قتل کر دیا تو مقتول کے اولیا کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ بھی ویسے کریں جیسے قاتل نے کیا ہے۔ تا کہ مثل کامل پر عمل ہو جائے لیکن اگر وہ صرف قتل کریں تو بھی جائز ہے مگر صاحبین کے نزدیک اولیا صرف قتل کے ذریعے سے قصاص لیں گے اور قطع یعنی چھوٹی جنایت کا قتل یعنی بڑی جنایت ہی میں مداخل ہو جائیگا۔ اصل میں اس مسئلے کی آٹھ صورتیں بنتی ہیں کیونکہ یا تو قطع اور قتل دونوں عمداً ہوں گے یا دونوں خطاء ہوں گے یا قطع عمدا اور قتل خطاء یا قطع خطاء اور قتل عمد ہو پھر ان چاروں صورتوں میں یا تو مقتول کا قتل قبل از تندرستی کے بعد ہوگا یا نہیں اگر قتل تندرستی سے قبل ہوا ہے اور دونوں خواہ عمداً ہوں یا دونوں خطاء ہوں تو بالاتفاق تداخل نہ ہوگا اور قتل تندرستی سے پہلے ہو تو اگر ان میں ایک عمداً ہوا اور ایک خطاء ہو تو پھر بھی تداخل نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر قتل تندرستی کے بعد ہوا اور دونوں خطاء ہوں تو بالاتفاق تداخل ہو گا اور اگر دونوں عمداً ہوں تو اس میں امام صاحب رحمہ اللہ اور صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے جیسا کہ متن میں ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ مزید وضاحت کے لئے یہ نقشہ ملاحظہ فرمائیں۔ تذکرہ قوله : ولا يضمن المثلى بالقيمة اذا انقطع المثل الا يوم الخصومة دوسری تفریح اس بارے میں کہ مثل صوری معنوی سے مقدم ہوتی ہے۔ امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر غاصب نے کوئی مثلی چیز غصب کر لی پھر اس کی مثل بازار سے منقطع ہوگئی تو غاصب پر یوم الخصومت کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اس لئے کہ جب تک قاضی کا فیصلہ نہیں ہو گا اس وقت تک امکان ہے کہ ہو سکتا ہے غاصب مثل صوری پر قادر ہو جائے۔ (۲) امام ابو یوسف کا مذہب : ... امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یوم الغصب کی قیمت کا اعتبار ہو گا اس لئے کہ جب تک اس کی مثل منقطع ہو گی تو اس کا حکم ذوات القیم والا ہوگا اور ذوات القیم میں تو یوم الغصب کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل کا رو اور جواب ہم کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں میں فرق ہے کیونکہ ذوات
He said: And the liability for life and limbs by property is an example of qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul: An example of qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul in the rights of people is that if someone is killed by mistake or his limbs are cut off by mistake, then the criminal is liable for the diyah and compensation for the limbs, even though there is no understood similarity between human life and limbs and property. He said: And giving the value when one marries for a slave not specified, even to compel acceptance as if he brought the named dower, is an example of qada' mushabil al-ada': An example of qada' mushabil al-ada' is that if a man marries a woman with an unspecified slave as dower, then if the husband buys a medium-quality slave and gives it to the wife, it becomes ada' (fulfillment), and if he gives its value, it becomes qada' (restitution) but in the sense of ada' because the criterion for ending the dispute between husband and wife regarding the slave is value. Because the higher-value slave would be 'a'la, the lower-value would be adna, and the medium-value would be mutawassit, or giving the value is also in the sense of ada'. Therefore the wife can be compelled to accept the value. The formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like. On this, Imam Sahib adduces two applications. The formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like. He said: And on this, Abu Hanifa said regarding cutting then intentional killing, the guardian may do both. (1) First application: If a tyrant first cuts someone's hand intentionally, then murders that injured person before recovery, the guardian of the victim has the option to do the same to the killer as the killer did. So that perfect resemblance is applied, but if he only kills, it is also permissible. However, according to the Sahibayn, the guardian will only take retaliation by killing, and the cutting (the lesser crime) merges into the killing (the greater crime). In reality, this issue has eight forms: either both cutting and killing are intentional, or both are mistaken, or cutting is intentional and killing mistaken, or cutting mistaken and killing intentional. Then in these four forms, either the killing occurs before or after recovery. If killing occurs before recovery and both are either intentional or both mistaken, there is no overlap by consensus. If killing before recovery and one is intentional and one mistaken, still there is no overlap. If killing after recovery and both are mistaken, by consensus there is overlap. If both are intentional, there is a disagreement between Imam Sahib and the Sahibayn as mentioned in the text. For more clarity, see this diagram. Reference. He said: And the mithli item is not liable by value until its like is cut off, except on the day of litigation. Second application regarding that the formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like. Imam Sahib says: If a usurper usurps a mithli item, then its like becomes unavailable on the market, then the usurper is liable for the value on the day of litigation, because until the judge issues a verdict, it is possible that the usurper may become able to get the formal like. (2) The school of Imam Abu Yusuf: According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the value on the day of usurpation will be considered, because once its like is cut off, its ruling becomes that of a qimi item, and for qimi items, the value on the day of usurpation is obligatory. The response of Imam Abu Yusuf's proof: We say there is a difference between them, because qimi items...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul work discusses the classification of restitution (qada') in Islamic law. The author begins by defining two types: 'qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul' (restitution with an unusual equivalent) and 'qada' mushabil al-ada'' (restitution resembling fulfillment). The first type applies when the thing owed has no natural monetary equivalent, like human life or limbs; here, mandatory compensation (diyah) is paid by money, even though no direct resemblance exists. The second type occurs when the obligation is to deliver a specific item, but the debtor gives its value instead; though technically a substitution, it is treated as fulfillment because the value is the measure of equivalence. The author then states a key principle: 'The formal like (مثل صوري) takes precedence over the meaningful like (مثل معنوي).' This means that when exact physical resemblance is possible, it must be pursued; only when impossible should one resort to value judgments. Two examples (tafri'at) from Imam Abu Hanifa illustrate this. First, in a case of intentional cutting followed by intentional killing before the victim recovers, the guardian of the victim may retaliate by both cutting and killing (the formal like) or just by killing (the meaningful like). The majority (Sahibayn) hold that the lesser crime merges into the greater, so only killing is due. The author notes eight possible scenarios based on intent and timing. Second, in a case of usurpation of a mithli (fungible) item whose like becomes unavailable, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the value is assessed at the time of litigation, because until the judge's decision, the like might become available again. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, assesses value at the time of usurpation, analogizing to qimi (non-fungible) items. The Hanafi response distinguishes the two, maintaining the priority of formal like.

Key Terms
مثل صوري (mithl suri): The formal like: a thing that resembles the original in outward form and genus, even if the specific quality differs.
مثل معنوي (mithl ma'nawi): The meaningful like: equivalence in value or utility, not in physical form.
قضاء بمثل غیر معقول (qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul): Restitution by an unusual equivalent: compensation in property for something that has no natural monetary measure (e.g., life, limbs).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'qada' mushabil al-ada'' according to the passage?
A) Paying diyah for accidental manslaughter.
B) Giving the value of a mithli item instead of the item itself, where the value is treated as fulfillment.
C) Restitution of a stolen unique painting by paying its market price.
D) Retaliation by cutting the hand of the perpetrator.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule 'al-mithl al-suri muqaddam ala al-mithl al-ma'nawi' governs restitution, prioritizing physical equivalence over value.

Applications of Formal Precedence: Cutting Then Killing and Valuation of Mithli Items
Applies the formal precedence rule to two specific legal disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of formal likeness to the two cases of (1) retaliation for cutting followed by killing and (2) valuation timing of a mithli item when its like becomes unavailable, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi disputes.

Core Idea
Formal precedence leads Imam Abu Hanifa to allow both cutting and killing in retaliation, and to assess value at litigation, while the Sahibayn merge the injuries and Abu Yusuf values at usurpation.

The general principle of formal likeness is not merely theoretical; it directly shapes rulings in retaliation and property compensation. This lesson explores two applications (tafri'at) from Imam Abu Hanifa, revealing how the principle is tested and refined through disagreements with the Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the first application scenario
Read the case: a person intentionally cuts another's hand, then intentionally kills that same victim before recovery. The guardian (wali) of the victim seeks retaliation.
This involves two distinct injuries: cutting and killing.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling and the Sahibayn's opposition
Abu Hanifa holds that the guardian may choose to retaliate by both cutting the killer's hand and then killing him (to achieve full formal equivalence), or by killing alone. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only killing is due; the cutting merges into the killing.
The principle 'formal like over meaningful like' is Abu Hanifa's basis.
3
Analyse the eight sub-cases
The text notes that this issue has eight forms depending on whether the acts were intentional or mistaken, and whether the killing occurred before or after recovery. Understand the consensus and disagreement in each sub-case.
Focus on the intentional + intentional after recovery case, where the dispute arises.
4
State the second application scenario
Read: a usurper (ghasib) seizes a mithli (fungible) item, and then its like becomes unavailable on the market. The question is when to assess the value for liability.
Mithli items are those measured by weight, volume, or count of similar units.
5
Compare Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on valuation timing
Abu Hanifa assesses value on the day of litigation because formal like might become available later. Abu Yusuf assesses on the day of usurpation because once unavailable, the item becomes qimi (value-based) and qimi items are valued at usurpation. The text rebuts Abu Yusuf by distinguishing the two situations.
This illustrates that formal precedence can affect procedural timing.

He said: And the liability for life and limbs by property is an example of qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul: An example of qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul in the rights of people is that if someone is killed by mistake or his limbs are cut off by mistake, then the criminal is liable for the diyah and compensation for the limbs, even though there is no understood similarity between human life and limbs and property. He said: And giving the value when one marries for a slave not specified, even to compel acceptance as if he brought the named dower, is an example of qada' mushabil al-ada': An example of qada' mushabil al-ada' is that if a man marries a woman with an unspecified slave as dower, then if the husband buys a medium-quality slave and gives it to the wife, it becomes ada' (fulfillment), and if he gives its value, it becomes qada' (restitution) but in the sense of ada' because the criterion for ending the dispute between husband and wife regarding the slave is value. Because the higher-value slave would be 'a'la, the lower-value would be adna, and the medium-value would be mutawassit, or giving the value is also in the sense of ada'. Therefore the wife can be compelled to accept the value. The formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like. On this, Imam Sahib adduces two applications. The formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like. He said: And on this, Abu Hanifa said regarding cutting then intentional killing, the guardian may do both. (1) First application: If a tyrant first cuts someone's hand intentionally, then murders that injured person before recovery, the guardian of the victim has the option to do the same to the killer as the killer did. So that perfect resemblance is applied, but if he only kills, it is also permissible. However, according to the Sahibayn, the guardian will only take retaliation by killing, and the cutting (the lesser crime) merges into the killing (the greater crime). In reality, this issue has eight forms: either both cutting and killing are intentional, or both are mistaken, or cutting is intentional and killing mistaken, or cutting mistaken and killing intentional. Then in these four forms, either the killing occurs before or after recovery. If killing occurs before recovery and both are either intentional or both mistaken, there is no overlap by consensus. If killing before recovery and one is intentional and one mistaken, still there is no overlap. If killing after recovery and both are mistaken, by consensus there is overlap. If both are intentional, there is a disagreement between Imam Sahib and the Sahibayn as mentioned in the text. For more clarity, see this diagram. Reference. He said: And the mithli item is not liable by value until its like is cut off, except on the day of litigation. Second application regarding that the formal like is given precedence over the meaningful like. Imam Sahib says: If a usurper usurps a mithli item, then its like becomes unavailable on the market, then the usurper is liable for the value on the day of litigation, because until the judge issues a verdict, it is possible that the usurper may become able to get the formal like. (2) The school of Imam Abu Yusuf: According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the value on the day of usurpation will be considered, because once its like is cut off, its ruling becomes that of a qimi item, and for qimi items, the value on the day of usurpation is obligatory. The response of Imam Abu Yusuf's proof: We say there is a difference between them, because qimi items...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul work discusses the classification of restitution (qada') in Islamic law. The author begins by defining two types: 'qada' bi mithl ghayr ma'qul' (restitution with an unusual equivalent) and 'qada' mushabil al-ada'' (restitution resembling fulfillment). The first type applies when the thing owed has no natural monetary equivalent, like human life or limbs; here, mandatory compensation (diyah) is paid by money, even though no direct resemblance exists. The second type occurs when the obligation is to deliver a specific item, but the debtor gives its value instead; though technically a substitution, it is treated as fulfillment because the value is the measure of equivalence. The author then states a key principle: 'The formal like (مثل صوري) takes precedence over the meaningful like (مثل معنوي).' This means that when exact physical resemblance is possible, it must be pursued; only when impossible should one resort to value judgments. Two examples (tafri'at) from Imam Abu Hanifa illustrate this. First, in a case of intentional cutting followed by intentional killing before the victim recovers, the guardian of the victim may retaliate by both cutting and killing (the formal like) or just by killing (the meaningful like). The majority (Sahibayn) hold that the lesser crime merges into the greater, so only killing is due. The author notes eight possible scenarios based on intent and timing. Second, in a case of usurpation of a mithli (fungible) item whose like becomes unavailable, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the value is assessed at the time of litigation, because until the judge's decision, the like might become available again. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, assesses value at the time of usurpation, analogizing to qimi (non-fungible) items. The Hanafi response distinguishes the two, maintaining the priority of formal like.

Key Terms
مثل صوري (mithl suri): The formal like: a thing that resembles the original in outward form and genus, even if the specific quality differs.
مداخل (mudakhal): Merging: the legal concept that a lesser crime or injury is absorbed into a greater one when they are connected.
مثلي (mithli): A fungible item that has equivalents in the market, measured by weight, volume, or count.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of intentional cutting followed by intentional killing after recovery, what is the ruling according to the Sahibayn?
A) The guardian may choose to retaliate by both cutting and killing.
B) The guardian must retaliate by cutting first, then killing.
C) The guardian may only retaliate by killing; the cutting is merged.
D) The guardian cannot retaliate at all; diyah must be accepted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi internal disputes demonstrate that the application of formal precedence is not absolute but weighed against other legal considerations like absorption and analogy to qimi items.

Tadakhul in Qisas: Intersection of Amputation and Killing
Ruling on tadakhul in qisas for amputation followed by killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scenarios in which tadakhul (merging) applies in qisas for amputation followed by killing, and explain the ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When amputation and killing are both intentional and occur after recovery, tadakhul is disputed—Imam Abu Hanifa allows both punishments, the Sahibayn merge them.

In qisas, when a person amputates a limb and then kills the victim, a question arises: do both punishments (amputation and killing) merge into one, or can the heirs exact both? This passage explores eight scenarios based on intention and timing, highlighting a key ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Understanding the nuances of tadakhul is essential for correctly applying qisas in complex cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
The passage addresses whether the punishment for amputation (cutting a limb) merges into the punishment for killing when both are inflicted successively on the same victim. The heirs' options depend on this merging.
2
Map the eight scenarios
The text outlines four categories based on intention (both intentional, both accidental, amputation intentional & killing accidental, amputation accidental & killing intentional) and two timing categories (killing before recovery or after recovery). List all combinations.
This yields 4 × 2 = 8 distinct scenarios.
3
Determine the ruling per scenario
For each scenario, apply the consensus or dispute as stated: if killing before recovery, no tadakhul; if after recovery, then if both accidental, tadakhul by consensus; if both intentional, ikhtilaf; if one intentional and one accidental, no tadakhul.
The passage explicitly mentions consensus in some cases and dispute in others.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf
When both acts are intentional and killing occurs after recovery, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the heirs may choose to amputate and kill (or just kill), while the Sahibayn hold that amputation merges into killing, so only killing is possible.
This is the main point of disagreement. The text does not provide evidence for either view.
5
Derive the usul principle
The underlying principle of tadakhul: when two punishments are for similar acts, the greater punishment may absorb the lesser. The dispute concerns whether intentional acts allow separation of punishments.
This connects to broader usul debates about the scope of tadakhul in Islamic criminal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہونے سے پہلے قتل کر دیا تو مقتول کے اولیا کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ بھی دیسے کریں جیسے قاتل نے کیا ہے۔ تا کہ مثل کامل پر عمل ہو جائے لیکن اگر وہ صرف قتل کریں تو بھی جائز ہے مگر صاحبین کے نزدیک اولیا صرف قتل کے ذریعے سے قصاص لیں گے اور قطع یعنی چھوٹی جنایت کا قتل یعنی برای جنایت ہی میں مذ اخل ہو جائیگا۔ اصل میں اس مسئلے کی آٹھ صورتیں بنتی ہیں کیونکہ یا تو قطع اور قتل دونوں عمد ا ہو نگے یا دونوں خطاء ہونگے یا قطع عمدا اور قتل خطام یا قطع خطاء اور قتل عمد ہو پھر ان چاروں صورتوں میں یا تو مقتول کا قتل قبل سے تندرستی کے بعد ہوگا یا نہیں اگر قتل تندرستی سے قبل ہوا ہے اور دونوں خواہ عمد ا ہوں یا دونوں خطاء ہوں تو بالاتفاق تداخل نہ ہوگا اور قتل تندرستی سے پہلے ہو تو اگر ان میں ایک عمد ا ہوا اور ایک خطا نہ ہو تو پھر بھی تداخل نہیں گا۔ اور اگر قتل تندرستی کے بعد ہوا اور دونوں خطاء ہوں تو با لا تفاق تداخل ہو گا اور اگر در ادوں عمد آہوں تو اس میں امام صاحب رحمہ اللہ اور صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے جیسا کہ متن میں ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ مزید وضاحت کے لئے یہ نقشہ ملاحظہ فرمائیں۔ تذکرہ
If he kills before recovery, the heirs of the slain have the option to do as the killer did, so that complete likeness is carried out. But if they only kill, it is also permissible. However, according to the two companions, the heirs will only exact qisas by killing, and the amputation (minor injury) will merge into the killing (major injury). In reality, eight scenarios arise in this issue: either both amputation and killing are intentional, or both are accidental, or amputation intentional and killing accidental, or amputation accidental and killing intentional. Then in each of these four cases, either the killing occurred before recovery from the injury or not. If the killing occurred before recovery and both were intentional or both accidental, then by consensus there is no merging. If the killing is before recovery and one is intentional and the other accidental, then also no merging. If the killing occurs after recovery and both are accidental, then by consensus merging occurs. If both are intentional, then there is a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) and the two companions, as mentioned in the text. For further clarification, refer to the diagram.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage discusses a ruling on qisas when a person first cuts off a limb (amputation) and then kills the victim before the wound heals. The heirs of the victim have the right to retaliate exactly as the killer did (i.e., cut and kill) or simply kill. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the heirs may choose to do both, but according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), only killing is allowed, as the amputation merges into the killing. The text then enumerates eight scenarios based on intention (intentional or accidental) and timing (before or after recovery). The ruling on merging (tadakhul) varies: if both acts are accidental and killing occurs after recovery, merging occurs by consensus. If both are intentional after recovery, there is a dispute. The passage concludes by referring to a diagram for clarity.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Merging of two punishments into one, such as amputation merging into killing in qisas
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment equal to the injury inflicted

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, how do the heirs exact qisas when amputation and killing occur before recovery?
A) They must amputate and kill
B) They can only kill, and amputation merges
C) They can only amputate
D) They have no right to qisas

Key Takeaway
When both amputation and killing are intentional and occur after recovery, tadakhul is disputed; otherwise, consensus determines no tadakhul if before recovery, and tadakhul if both accidental after recovery.

Valuation of Mithli When Like is Unavailable: A Three-Way Dispute
Dispute on valuation date for mithli when like unavailable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on the valuation date of a mithli item when its like is unavailable, and apply the tarjih mechanism to determine the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When a mithli item's like is cut off, the valuation date is disputed: day of litigation (Abu Hanifa), day of usurpation (Abu Yusuf), or day of cutoff (Muhammad).

In this lesson, we delve into a classic Hanafi dispute on the compensation for usurped fungible goods. When the market like of a stolen item dries up, on what date should its value be assessed? The three senior Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—offer different answers. Understanding their reasoning sharpens your grasp of analogical reasoning (qiyas) and the role of possibility in legal rulings, especially in the context of damages (daman).

Guided Path
1
Identify the common ground
All three imams agree that a mithli item is guaranteed by its like; only when the like becomes unavailable does the question of valuation arise. The dispute is over which date to use for that valuation.
2
State the three positions and their proofs
Abu Hanifa: day of litigation, because the usurper might still produce the like until the judge's ruling. Abu Yusuf: day of usurpation, by analogy to qimi items. Muhammad: day of cutoff, because inability is established then.
3
Analyze the Hanafi responses to the Sahibayn
The text rebuts Abu Yusuf by noting that for qimi items, value is only due after inability to return the like, not immediately. It rebuts Muhammad by arguing that inability becomes apparent only in court, so litigation day is decisive.
4
Determine the tarjih and practical consequence
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's: valuation on the day of litigation. This ensures that the usurper cannot benefit from delay and the victim receives fair compensation based on the latest market conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا يضمن المثلى بالقيمة اذا انقطع المثل الا يوم الخصومة دوسری تفریح اس بارے میں کہ مثل صوری معنوی سے مقدم ہوتی ہے۔ امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر غاصب نے کوئی مثلی چیز غصب کر لی پھر اس کی مثل بازار سے منقطع ہوگئی تو غاصب پر یوم الخصومت کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اس لئے کہ جب تک قاضی کا فیصلہ نہیں ہو گا اس وقت تک امکان ہے کہ ہو سکتا ہے نا صب مثل صوری پر قادر ہو جائے۔ (۲) امام ابو یوسف کا مذہب : ... امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یوم الغصب کی قیمت کا اعتبار ہو گا اس لئے کہ جب تک اس کی مثل منقطع ہو گی تو اس کا حکم ذات القیم والا ہوگا اور ذوات القیم میں تو یوم الغصب کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل کا رو اور جواب ہم کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں میں فرق ہے کیونکہ ذوات القیم میں حکم یہ تھا کہ اصل کو واپس کرے اگر اصل واپس ہو سکے اگر اصل واپس نہیں کر سکتا تو مثل اپس کرے اگر مثل سے بھی عاجز ہو تو پھر قیمت ہی واجب ہوگی۔ (۳) امام محمد کامذہب امام محمد کے نزدیک یوم الانقطاع کی قیمت کا اعتبار ہوگا کیونکہ اصل کے دینے سے عاجز ہونا اسی دن ثابت ہو گا۔ امام محمد کی دلیل کارو... ہم کہتے ہیں عاجز ہونا تو اس دن ثابت ہو گا مگر عجز ظاہر اس وقت ہوگا جب قاضی کے سامنے مقدمہ پیش ہو گا لہذا خصومت والے روز کا ہی اعتبار کیا جائے گا۔ یہاں سے ایک اور اصول سمجھ میں آتا ہے وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی چیز کی مثل نہ ہو نہ مشکل کامل، اور نہ مثل قاصر ، اور نہ مثل صوری اور نہ مشکل معنوی تو اس کا ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ جب مثل معدوم ہو تو منمان واجب ہوگا۔ اس پر مصنف نے تین تفریعات اٹھا ئیں ہیں۔
He said: And a mithli (fungible) is not guaranteed by value when its like is cut off except on the day of litigation. Second derivation: concerning that the formal like takes precedence over the semantic like. Imam Abu Hanifa says: If a usurper seizes a mithli item and then its like becomes unavailable in the market, then the value on the day of litigation is obligatory upon the usurper, because until the judge gives his decision, there is possibility that the usurper may become able to produce a formal like. (2) The school of Imam Abu Yusuf: ... According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the value on the day of usurpation is considered, because when its like becomes unavailable, its ruling becomes like that of items of value (qimi), and for items of value, the value on the day of usurpation is obligatory. The response to Imam Abu Yusuf's proof: We say there is a difference because in items of value, the ruling was that he should return the original if possible; if he cannot return the original, then the like; if he is also unable to give the like, then value becomes obligatory. (3) The school of Imam Muhammad: According to Imam Muhammad, the value on the day of cutoff (when like becomes unavailable) is considered, because inability to give the original is established on that day. The response to Imam Muhammad's proof: We say inability is established on that day, but the inability becomes apparent only when the case is presented before the judge; therefore, the day of litigation is considered. From here another principle is understood: that if an item has no like – neither a full difficult like, nor an inferior like, nor a formal like, nor a semantic difficult like – then its guarantee is obligatory. When the like is non-existent, guarantee is obligatory. The author raised three derivations on this.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among the three Hanafi imams regarding the date on which the value of a mithli (fungible) item is assessed when its market like becomes unavailable. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that value is assessed on the day of litigation (yawm al-khusuma) because the usurper might still be able to produce the like until the judge's ruling. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that value is assessed on the day of usurpation (yawm al-ghasb) because once the like is unavailable, the item becomes like a qimi (valuable item) for which the day of usurpation is the benchmark. Imam Muhammad holds that value is assessed on the day the like becomes unavailable (yawm al-inqita') because inability is established on that day. The text then provides responses to the latter two views, ultimately supporting the view of Abu Hanifa: the inability becomes apparent only when the case is before the judge, so the day of litigation is the correct date. The passage then states a general usul principle: when all categories of like (formal, semantic, etc.) are absent, guarantee of the value is due. The author (Muhammad) then derives three sub-issues from this principle.

Key Terms
مثلی (mithli): An item that has close equivalents in the market, e.g., grains, oils
قیمی (qimi): An item that has no close equivalent, assessed by value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, on which date is the value of a mithli item assessed when its like becomes unavailable?
A) Day of usurpation
B) Day of litigation
C) Day of cutoff of like
D) Day of return of item

Key Takeaway
The valuation date for a mithli item when its like is unavailable is the day of litigation according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Three Categories of Liability in Usurpation (Ghasb)
Listing and comparing three categories of liability in usurpation (ghasb).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on daman for the three categories in ghasb and explain the basis for the ikhtilaf with Shafi'i regarding manafi.

Core Idea
In ghasb, the principal is always compensable, increments are not, and usufruct is not compensable in Hanafi law—contrary to Shafi'i.

This passage from Khulasat al-Anwar outlines the Hanafi rule on liability (daman) in usurpation (ghasb). The author lists three categories—the usurped object itself, its increments (e.g., milk, offspring), and its usufruct (manafi)—and explains which incur daman and which do not. Additionally, a key inter-madhhab dispute is highlighted: the Shafi'i school holds that usufruct is compensable based on custom (urf). Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi concept of mutaqawwim property and the role of custom in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the text, list: (1) the usurped object itself (maghsab nafsahu), (2) its increments (zawaid) like milk and offspring, and (3) its usufruct (manafi).
The categories are distinct in their daman rulings.
2
Determine daman for each category per Hanafis
For the principal, daman is wajib whether destroyed or consumed. For increments, no daman for either destruction or use. For usufruct, no daman in any case.
Note the matn's terms: talf (destruction) vs. itlaf (consumption).
3
Compare Hanafi and Shafi'i on manafi
Shafi'i holds that manafi are compensable based on custom (urf). Hanafis respond that urf does not create liability against a usurper; only mutaqawwim property is compensable.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in fatwa.
4
Derive the underlying usul principle
The Hanafi position rests on the principle that daman requires the object to be mutaqawwim (having stable value). Manafi lack taqawwum. Increments are attached to the principal but not compensable separately.
Shafi'i uses qiyas to ijarah and urf to classify manafi as mal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور زائد بھی واجب ہوتا ہے لیکن غصب کی صورت میں رضا مندی نہیں ہے۔ لیکن امام شافعی کے نزدیک عرف کے مطابق منافع کا ضمان واجب ہو گا
(1)
پھر یہاں تین چیزیں ہیں:
مغصوب نفسہ اس کی ضمان ہلاک اور استھلاک دونوں سے واجب ہوگی
(۲) زوائد جیسے جانور کا دودھ اور بچے وغیرہ ان کی ضمان نہ ہلاک سے
آئے گی اور نہ افلاک سے آئیگی جیسے گھوڑے پر سواری کرنا۔
(۳) منافع ... احناف کے ہاں ضمان نہیں ہے اور شوافع کے ہاں ضمان واجب ہے احناف کے ہاں بلاک کی صورت میں جیسے ماتن نے تلف سے تعبیر کیا ہے اور نہ استعملاک کی صورت میں جسے ماتن : اتلاف سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔
And extra is also wajib, but in case of usurpation there is no consent. However, according to Imam Shafi'i, according to custom, the guarantee of profits is wajib. (1) Then three things are here: The usurped thing itself: its guarantee is wajib from both destruction and consumption. (2) Increments like the milk of an animal and offspring etc.: their guarantee does not come from destruction nor from consumption, like riding a horse. (3) Profits: According to Hanafis, there is no guarantee, and according to Shafi'is, guarantee is wajib. According to Hanafis, in the case of destruction (talf) as the matn expressed, and not in the case of use (istihlak) as the matn expressed as itlaf.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a comparative ruling on the three categories of liability (daman) in usurpation (ghasb). The author explains that the usurped object itself (maghsab nafsahu) always incurs daman, whether destroyed or consumed. The increments (zawaid), such as milk or offspring, do not incur daman in either case. As for usufruct (manafi), the Hanafi position is that there is no daman, while Imam Shafi'i holds that daman is wajib based on custom (urf). The author distinguishes between talf (destruction) and itlaf (consumption), noting that the matn uses these terms respectively.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.
منافع (manafi): Usufruct or benefits derived from an object.
غصب (ghasb): Taking another's property forcibly and without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person usurps a car and drives it without permission, does he owe compensation for the use (usufruct)?
A) Yes, because he benefited.
B) No, because usufruct is not mutaqawwim.
C) Yes, because custom requires it.
D) No, because he did not destroy the car.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability in ghasb covers the principal object only, not increments or usufruct; the Shafi'i school differs on usufruct based on custom.

Qisas and Daman: The 'No Equivalent, No Guarantee' Principle
Ruling on liability for loss of qisas when murderer is killed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a right without a monetary equivalent (mithl) does not support a claim for compensation (daman), using the case of qisas as an example.

Core Idea
What has no equivalent cannot be compensated; qisas is a right, not a commodity.

This lesson examines a key usul principle and its application: if a stranger kills a murderer sentenced to qisas, the original victim's heirs cannot claim compensation from the stranger. The Hanafi school grounds this on the rule that only property-like items (mutaqawwim) carry guarantee. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, analogizing to diyah. We'll deconstruct the reasoning and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
Find the maxim stated at the beginning: 'whatever has no equivalent has no guarantee.' Understand that this is the foundational rule for the entire discussion.
The principle is general and applies across fiqh.
2
Analyze the tafri' (branch)
See how the author applies the principle: a stranger kills the murderer. The heirs of the first victim cannot claim from the stranger because qisas is not mutaqawwim. Note who is making the claim and against whom.
The stranger is not the murderer; he is a third party.
3
Examine the Shafi'i counterargument
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since diyah is wajib for accidental killing, qisas must be valuable. Identify the analogy he draws.
Diyah is a monetary substitute for life.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes diyah as a legislative concession, not evidence of qisas's value. Also note that the stranger's act benefits the heirs by killing their enemy.
Benefit does not create liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi – no liability; Shafi'i – the stranger must pay the value of qisas (e.g., diyah).
This affects financial outcomes in similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والقصاص لا يضمن بقتل القاتل
مصنف نے جو اصول بیان کیا کہ جس چیز کی مثل نہیں اس کی ضمان بھی نہیں اس پر دوسری تفریح یہ اٹھاتے ہیں کہ اگر قاتل کو کسی اجنبی شخص نے قتل کر دیا تو مقتول اول کے ورثاء اس قاتل سے مہمان کا مطالبہ نہیں کریں گے اگر چہ مقتول ثانی کے ورثاء اس اجنبی سے ضمان کا مطالبہ کر سکتے ہیں۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ قصاص کوئی متقوم چیز نہیں اور اس کی کوئی مثل بھی نہیں لہذا قصاص کے ضائع ہونے سے اجنبی پر کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں آئیگا ۔ امام شافعی کا مذہب ... امام شافع کے نزدیک یہ اجنبی ضامن ہوگا ۔ اس لئے سر قتل خطا میں دیت واجب ہوتی ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص متقوم چیز ہے۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل کارو ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ قتل خطاء کے لئے کوئی مثل نہیں تا ہم مسلمان کے خون کو صدرہونے سے بچانے کیلئے ہم نے دیت کو واجب کیا لیکن یہاں پر اجنبی نے مقتول کے ورثاء کا کوئی نقصان نہیں کیا بلکہ ان کے دشمن کو قتل کر کے ان کی مدد کی ہے لہذا ضمان واجب نہیں ہوگا۔
His saying: 'And qisas is not guaranteed by the killing of the killer.' The author, having stated the principle that whatever has no equivalent has no guarantee, derives a second branch: if a stranger kills the murderer, the heirs of the first victim will not claim compensation from that murderer, although the heirs of the second victim can claim guarantee from the stranger. The reason is that qisas is not a valuable thing (mutaqawwim) and has no equivalent; therefore, its loss does not impose guarantee on the stranger. Imam Shafi'i's school: according to Imam Shafi'i, this stranger is liable. Because in unintentional killing, blood money (diyah) is wajib, so it is known that qisas is a valuable thing. We respond to Imam Shafi'i's proof: unintentional killing has no equivalent, yet we made diyah wajib to prevent bloodshed of Muslims; but here the stranger did not harm the heirs, rather he helped them by killing their enemy, so guarantee is not wajib.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a branch (tafri') of the usul principle that what has no equivalent (mithl) carries no guarantee. The author applies it to qisas: if the murderer (qatil) is killed by a stranger, the first victim's heirs cannot claim anything from that stranger because qisas is not property (mutaqawwim). Imam Shafi'i argues that since diyah is given for accidental killing, qisas must have value; the Hanafi response distinguishes between the two: diyah is a legislative concession to prevent bloodshed, not an indication that qisas has value. The stranger actually assisted the heirs by killing their enemy.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation bodily punishment for murder or injury.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Valuable in the sense of having a lawful equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a stranger kills a murderer before qisas is carried out, what are the stranger's obligations to the victim's heirs?
A) He must pay full diyah.
B) He is liable for qisas value.
C) No obligation.
D) He must pay half of diyah.

Key Takeaway
Qisas is a right of retaliation, not a property right; therefore, its loss by a third party's act does not create a liability for compensation.
