Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Mutashabihat: Where to Pause
Dispute on interpretation of Quran 3:7

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the interpretation of mutashabihat and apply the Hanafi usul principle regarding waqf and conjunction to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
Where you pause in the verse determines who knows the interpretation of the ambiguous verses.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute centered on Quran 3:7, which discusses mutashabihat—verses whose meanings are ambiguous. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on whether those firmly rooted in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-ilm) also know the interpretation. At stake is the method of interpreting ambiguous texts, a cornerstone of Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Verse
Locate the verse 'What ya'lamu ta'wilah illa Allah wa al-rasikhun fi al-ilm yaquluna amanna bih'. Note the grammatical structure: the word 'wa' could be a conjunction connecting 'al-rasikhun' to 'Allah' as joint subjects of 'ya'lamu', or a separate clause.
The verse is Q. 3:7.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Position
Hanafis consider it obligatory (wajib) to stop at 'illa Allah', making 'wa al-rasikhun' the start of a new sentence. Thus, 'al-rasikhun' are not subjects of 'ya'lamu', but rather they 'say we believe in it'. This is supported by a recitation without the waw (al-rasikhun) and another where 'yaqulu al-rasikhun' is used instead of 'yaquluna'.
The waw-less recitation and the singular verb indicate a new sentence.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i connects 'wa al-rasikhun' to 'illa Allah' as a conjunction, making them joint subjects of 'ya'lamu'. Therefore, the meaning is that Allah and those deeply rooted in knowledge know the interpretation. The stop is at 'al-rasikhun fi al-ilm'.
The default rule of conjunction (al-'atf) supports attaching the waw to the previous clause.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi Rebuttal and Verbal Nature of Dispute
The Hanafi response is that even if the learned have some understanding, they do not have definitive (qat'i) knowledge of the ambiguous. The objection that ambiguous verses would be pointless is answered: they serve to test the believer—the ignorant are commanded to acquire knowledge, and the scholars are tested by refraining from excessive speculation.
Thus, the practical difference (thamarah) is whether scholars may independently interpret mutashabihat or must affirm without certainty.
5
Apply the Principle to a New Example
Consider other mutashabihat, such as verses about divine attributes. Hanafis affirm them without ta'wil (figurative interpretation), while Shafi'is may permit interpretation. The same usul principle applies: stop at the point where certainty ends.
This demonstrates the ongoing relevance of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف کا منشاء اللہ تعالیٰ کا یہ قول ہے۔ وما يعلم تأويله الا الله والراسخون فی العلم يقولون آمنا به - ہمارے نزدیک (الا الله ) پر وقف کرنا واجب ہے اور (والراسخون ) نیا جملہ ہے اس لئے کہ اللہ تعالی نے گمراہوں کا حصہ یہ بتلایا کہوہ متشابھات کے پیچھے پڑتے ہیں اور دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ ہم ایک قرأت بدون الواو (الراسخون ) ہے۔ ایک دوسری قراۃ میں (والرسخون يقولون ) کی بجائے ( و يقول الراسخون ) ہے جا مانع للعطف ہے لہزار اسکین کو (آمنا به ) کا قائل ماننا ہوگا۔ امام شافعی کے نزدیک (والر اسخون ) کا عطف ہے ( الا الله ) پراور (والد سخون فی العلم ) پر وقف ہے لہذا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ راشین فی العلم کو متشابھات کا علم ہے۔ بعض حضرات کا کہنا ہے کہ حقیقت میں یہ سارا اختلاف لفظی ہے اسلئے کہ امام شافعی وغیرہ کہتے ہیں کہ راتین فی اعلم کو متشابھات کاظمی علم ہوتا ہے اور ہم کہتے ہیں کہ انہیں ان کا قطعی علم نہیں ہوتا۔ اعتراض ... متشابھات کے نزول کا کیا فائدہ جب کسی کو اس کی مراد معلوم ہی نہیں ہے؟ جواب ہم کہتے ہیں کہ اس کا فائدہ بندے کی آزمائش کرنا ہے اس لئے کہ ہر شخص کی آزمائش اس کی تمنا کے خلاف ہوتی ہے تو جاہل کی آزمائش یہ ہے کہ اسے علم سکھنے کا حکم دیا جائے اور عالم کی آزمائش یہ ہیکہ اسے علم مثلاً متشابھات وغیرہ میں غور وفکر کرنے سے روک دیا جائے ۔
The basis of the difference is the statement of Allah: 'And no one knows its interpretation except Allah, and those firmly grounded in knowledge say: We believe in it.' According to us, it is obligatory to stop at 'except Allah', and 'and those firmly grounded' is a new sentence, because Allah described the share of the misguided as that they pursue the ambiguous. Secondly, there is a recitation without the waw: 'al-rasikhun'. In another recitation, instead of 'wal-rasikhuna yaquluna' it is 'wa yaqulu al-rasikhuna', which prevents conjunction. Thus, they must be considered saying 'We believe in it'. According to Imam Shafi'i, 'wal-rasikhuna' is conjuncted to 'illallah', and stopping is at 'wal-rasikhuna fi al-ilm', so the meaning becomes that those firmly grounded in knowledge have knowledge of the ambiguous. Some say that this entire difference is verbal, because Imam Shafi'i and others say that the firmly grounded have knowledge of the ambiguous, while we say they do not have definitive knowledge. Objection: What benefit is there in revealing the ambiguous if no one knows its intended meaning? Answer: We say its benefit is to test the servant, because every person's test is contrary to his desire. So the test of the ignorant is to command him to learn knowledge, and the test of the scholar is to prevent him from pondering over knowledge, e.g., the ambiguous.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute regarding the interpretation of the ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat). The Hanafi position is that stopping at 'illallah' in the verse is obligatory, making 'wal-rasikhuna' the start of a new independent clause, indicating that only Allah knows the definitive interpretation. The Shafi'i view connects 'wal-rasikhuna' to 'illallah', meaning that the learned also know the interpretation. The passage then addresses an objection about the purpose of ambiguous verses, answering that it serves as a test for believers.

Key Terms
متشابہ (mutashabih): Verses or words whose intended meaning is unclear or ambiguous, requiring interpretation.
وقف (waqf): The pause in reciting a verse.
راسخون فی العلم (al-rasikhun fi al-ilm): Those deeply rooted in knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the function of 'wal-rasikhuna' in the verse?
A) It is a continuation of the subject of 'ya'lamu', meaning the learned also know the interpretation.
B) It is the subject of a new sentence, meaning they say 'We believe in it'.
C) It is a separated phrase with no grammatical function.
D) It is an exclamation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that only Allah possesses definitive knowledge of the interpretation of mutashabihat, while scholars affirm faith without claiming certainty.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on 'Umum al-Majaz
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i claim that majaz is necessarily limited; example from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'umum al-majaz, identify the Shafi'i objection, and apply the Hanafi rebuttal to a textual example from hadith.

Core Idea
Majaz can be general when indicators of generality are present; necessity pertains to the listener, not the speaker.

This lesson examines the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over whether metaphorical language (majaz) can be general (umum). The Hanafi school holds that the same linguistic indicators that produce generality in literal language also produce generality in metaphor. The Shafi'i school restricts majaz to necessity. We will explore both arguments and see how they apply to a prophetic hadith on selling sa' for sa's.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on 'umum al-majaz
The Hanafi school affirms that majaz can be general (umum) when additional indicators of generality (e.g., plural form, indefinite in negation) are present. Generality is not inherent to haqiqah but arises from these indicators, so they apply equally to majaz.
This is the core claim to defend.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its basis
The Shafi'i school holds that majaz is used only out of necessity ('darurah') when the literal meaning is impossible. Therefore, it must be restricted to the extent of that necessity and cannot be general.
Note the difference: necessity as a limiting factor.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal using Quranic evidence
The Hanafis counter that the Quran contains abundant majaz, yet Allah is free from necessity. If majaz were limited by necessity, that would imply deficiency in Allah. Furthermore, the Shafi'i analogy with iqtida' (implied elements) fails because iqtida' relates to the mujtahid's inference, not the speaker.
The rebuttal shifts necessity from the speaker to the listener.
4
Apply the dispute to the hadith of Ibn Umar on sa'
In the hadith, 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams, nor a sa' for two sa's,' the word 'sa'' is used metaphorically for the food contained in the vessel. The Hanafis generalize 'sa'' to mean anything that fits in a sa', allowing the sale of the vessel itself. The Shafi'is restrict 'sa'' to 'food' by default.
This shows the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.
5
Contrast the two interpretations and note the thamarah
The Hanafi interpretation permits selling the container (sa') for two containers, while the Shafi'i interpretation prohibits it unless the food is explicitly mentioned. This demonstrates how acceptance or rejection of 'umum al-majaz changes the ruling.
Focus on the actual legal difference.

امام شافعی کی دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مجاز ضروری ہے یعنی ضرورت کی بناء پر اس کی طرف رجوع کیا جاتا ہے جب معنی حقیقی مراد لینا محمد رہو [؟] اور ضرورت بقدر ضرورت مقدر مانی جاتی ہے۔ الضرورة تنتقد ربقد رها [؟] . احناف کا مذہب احناف کے نزدیک عموم مجاز جائز ہے یعنی معنی کو اس قدر عام کر دیا جائے کہ اس میں حقیقی اور مجازی دونوں معنی آجائیں۔ احناف کی دلیل ہی کہتے ہیں کہ حقیقت میں جو عموم ہوتا ہے وہ حقیقت ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہوتا بلکہ کسی زائد دلالت کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے مثلاً جمع کا صیغہ ہونا، نکرہ کا سیاق نفی میں واقع ہونا وغیرہ وغیرہ۔ شوافع کو جواب... الہذا جب یہ دلالتیں مجاز میں پائی جائینگی تو وہ بھی عام ہو گا باقی آپ کا یہ کہنا کہ مجاز ضروری ہوتا ہے یہ صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ کتاب اللہ میں مجاز کثرت کے ساتھ آیا ہے ، حالانکہ اللہ تعالیٰ ضرورت سے پاک ہیں۔ اگر ہم مجاز کو ضرورت کی حد تک قرار دے دیں تو اللہ کے لئے ضرورت ثابت کرنالازم آئے گا جو بجز [؟] کی علامت ہے اور اللہ تعالیٰ بجز [؟] سے پاک ہے۔ اعتراض... شوافع احناف پر اعتراض کرتے ہیں کہ یہی بات تو مقتضیٰ کی صورت میں بھی لازم آتی ہے مقتضی قرآن مجید میں بکثرت واقع ہے حالانکہ بھی ضروری ہے۔ جواب ... مقتضاء استدال کی اقسام میں سے ہے لہذا وہاں ضرورت کا تعلق مجتہد کے ساتھ ہے تکلم کیسا تھ نہیں جو کہ اللہ تعالی ہے لہذا اللہ تعالیٰ کیلئے عجز و ضرورت ثابت نہیں ہوتا شارح فرماتے ہیں کہ انصاف کی بات یہ ہے کہ مجاز کا ضرورت ہونا بھی سامع کے اعتبار سے ہے۔ قوله : ولهذا جعلنا لفظ الصاع في حديث ابن عمر رضى الله عنه عا ما فيما يحله عموم مجاز کی ایک مثال : اسی وجہ سے ہم کہتے ہیں کہ چونکہ مجاز میں عموم ہوتا ہے لہذا حضرت عبداللہ ابن عمر رضی اللہ عنہما کی اس حدیث میں لا تبيعو الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين صاع ہر اس چیز کا نام ہے جو صاع میں حلول کرتی ہو برتن (صاع) کی بیچ دو برتنوں (صاع) کے ساتھ جائز ہے۔ تو امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ تعالیٰ یہاں لفظ طعام کو نکالتے ہیں گویا اصل عبارت یوں ہے لا تبيعوا الطعام الحال فى الصاع بالطعام الحال في الصاعين ۔ اور ہم یوں تقدیر نکلالتے ہیں کہ لا تبيعوا كل ما يحل في الصاع بالصاعين .
The gist of Imam Shafi'i's proof is that majaz is necessary, i.e., it is resorted to out of necessity when taking the literal meaning is impossible [؟], and necessity is estimated according to its extent. The necessity is limited to its measure [؟]. The Hanafi school: According to the Hanafis, generalization of majaz (umum al-majaz) is permissible, i.e., the meaning is generalized to such an extent that both literal and metaphorical meanings are included. The proof of the Hanafis is that the generality that occurs in haqiqah is not due to its being haqiqah, but due to some additional indicator, e.g., the plural form, the indefinite in a negative context, etc. Response to the Shafi'is: Therefore, when these indicators are present in majaz, it will also be general. As for your statement that majaz is necessary, that is not correct, because majaz occurs frequently in the Book of Allah, while Allah is free from necessity. If we limit majaz to the extent of necessity, then necessity would have to be proven for Allah, which is a sign of incapacity, and Allah is free from incapacity. Objection: The Shafi'is object to the Hanafis that the same thing applies in the case of iqtida' (implied meaning); iqtida' occurs abundantly in the Quran, yet it is also necessary. Response: Iqtida' is among the types of inference, so there the necessity relates to the mujtahid, not to the speaker, who is Allah; thus necessity and incapacity are not proven for Allah. The commentator says that the fair view is that the necessity of majaz is from the perspective of the listener. His statement: For this reason we have made the word 'sa'' in the hadith of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) general for whatever it contains. An example of generalization in majaz: For this reason we say that since generality exists in majaz, in this hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams, nor a sa' for two sa's,' the word sa' is the name of everything that contains a sa' (the vessel). So selling a vessel (sa') for two vessels (sa') is permissible. Imam Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) here removes the word 'food' (ta'am), as if the original wording is: 'Do not sell the food that is in a sa' for the food that is in two sa's.' And we estimate it as: 'Do not sell everything that is contained in a sa' for two sa's.'
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the permissibility of 'umum al-majaz' (generalization in metaphorical usage). The Shafi'i position, as summarized in the text, is that majaz is inherently restricted by necessity: one only resorts to metaphor when the literal meaning is impossible, and it is confined to the extent of that necessity. The Hanafi school counters that generality is not an inherent property of literal wording, but rather arises from specific linguistic indicators such as plural forms or indefinite nouns in negative contexts. Therefore, if these indicators are present in a metaphorical expression, it too can be general. The Hanafis further argue that the Shafi'i claim that majaz is necessitated by dire need is untenable because the Quran itself contains abundant metaphor, yet Allah is beyond all need and necessity. To claim otherwise would imply deficiency in Allah. The Shafi'i objection that the same logic applies to iqtida' (implied or deleted elements) is answered by distinguishing that iqtida' relates to the inference of the mujtahid, not the divine speaker. The commentator (sharh) adds a fair resolution: the necessity of majaz pertains to the listener's understanding, not to the speaker. The passage then illustrates the Hanafi application with the hadith of Ibn Umar prohibiting the sale of a sa' for two sa's. The Shafi'is interpret 'sa'' as 'food contained in a sa'', whereas the Hanafis interpret it as 'anything that is contained in a sa''—thus a general metaphorical usage of the container for the contained, allowing the sale of the vessel itself. This example shows how the Hanafi acceptance of 'umum al-majaz' leads to a wider application of the ruling.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage of a word for a meaning other than its original, due to a connection (علاقہ) and a preventer (مانع) from the literal meaning.
عموم مجاز (ʿumūm al-majāz): The generalization of a metaphorical expression to include both literal and metaphorical meanings due to the presence of indicators of generality.
ضرورت (ḍarūrah): Necessity that forces one to abandon the literal meaning and use figurative language.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof in this passage, what is the actual cause of generalization (عموم) in both haqiqah and majaz?
A) The fact that the word is used literally (حقیقت)
B) The presence of additional linguistic indicators such as plural form or indefinite in negation
C) The necessity (ضرورت) of the speaker
D) The absence of any preventing factor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits generalizing metaphorical language when linguistic indicators of generality are present, distinguishing between necessity for the speaker and for the listener.

Interpreting 'Yawm' in Hanafi Oath Rulings: Haqiqah, Majaz, and the Dabita
Analyzing a fourth objection on combining haqiqah and majaz in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule (dabita) for interpreting 'yawm' in conditional oaths and evaluate the objection regarding combining haqiqah and majaz.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'yawm' in oaths is interpreted as absolute time via majaz, not literal daytime, avoiding combination of haqiqah and majaz.

This lesson delves into a classic usul dispute: how should 'yawm' (day) be interpreted in an oath like 'My slave is free on the day Zaid arrives'? The objection claims that combining literal daytime and figurative night is impermissible. The Hanafi response offers a nuanced rule based on the nature of the actions involved. By mastering this discussion, you'll sharpen your ability to handle similar apparent conflicts between literal and figurative meanings in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the passage to pinpoint the exact objection against the Hanafi position: that using 'yawm' for both day and night combines haqiqah and majaz, which is prohibited.
The objector claims that 'yawm' literally means daytime (nahaar) and figuratively means night, and using it for both in one context is invalid.
2
State the Hanafi Position and Its Basis
The Hanafi response argues that 'yawm' here is used as a metaphor (majaz) for absolute time (waqt), which inherently includes both day and night. This is a single intended meaning, not a combination.
This avoids combining haqiqah and majaz because the metaphorical meaning is the sole intended one.
3
Analyze the Rule (Dabita) for Determining Meaning
Examine the rule presented: if both actions (the oath and the event) are extended (mumtadd), 'yawm' means daytime; if both are non-extended, it means absolute time; if one is extended and the other not, the verb of the agent (the one swearing) governs.
This rule is specific to Hanafi usul and reflects their method of reconciling literal and figurative uses.
4
Apply the Rule to the Dispute
In the given oath ('on the day he arrives'), the arrival is typically non-extended, and the oath itself is also non-extended. Thus, 'yawm' means absolute time, so the oath is broken whether arrival occurs day or night.
If both actions were extended (e.g., both are ongoing), 'yawm' would be limited to daytime.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هے خواہ وہ مافیا ( ننگے پیر ) ہو یا متعل (جوتے پہن کر) ہو ۔ ہاں اگر اس کی کوئی خاص نیت ہو تو اس کا اعتبار ہو گا۔ لیکن اگر کسی نے بغیر دخول کے قدم رکھا تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ یہ حقیقت مجبور ہ ہے اور دار فلاں یہ دار سکتی " جس گھر میں رہائش اختیار کی جائے" سے کنایہ ہے یہ خواہ وہ بطور ملک ہو یا اجارہ یا عاریت ۔ قوله : وانما يحنث اذا قدم ليلاً أونها رأفي قوله عبده حر يوم يقدم فلان لأن المراد با ليوم الوقت وهو عام. چوتھا اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی کہ عبدی حر یوم يقدم فلان تو اس کا حقیقی معنی تو یہ ہے کہ ( یوم) سے مراد صرف ( نہار ) ہو اور مجازی معنی یہ ہے کہ رات کیلئے استعمال ہو حالانکہ تم نے حقیقت اور مجاز کو جمع کر دیا اور تم نے کہا کہ فلاں جس وقت میں بھی آئے خواہ دن میں بارات میں بہر صورت غلام آزاد ہو جائیگا ۔ جواب ..... یوم سے عموم مجاز کے طریقہ پر صرف وقت مراد ہے جو دن اور رات دونوں کو شامل ہے۔ بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ یوم کا لفظ نہار اور مطلق وقت کے درمیان مشترک ہے اور یہاں مطلق وقت کا معنی مراد ہے۔ اور اس کیلئے ضابطہ بیان کیا گیا ہے کہ اگر دونوں فعل ممتد ہوں تو یوم سے مراد نہار ہو گا اور اگر دونوں غیر ممتد ہوں تو اس سے مراد وقت ہوگا اور اگر ایک ممتد ہو دوسرا نہ ہو تو اعتبار فعل عامل کا ہو گا اور فعل مضاف الیہ کا اعتبار نہیں ہو گا ۔
Whether he is barefoot or wearing shoes. Yes, if he has a specific intention, it will be considered. But if someone placed a foot without entering, it will not be considered, because this is a compelled reality and 'house' is a metaphor for 'a dwelling where one resides', whether by ownership, lease, or loan. He said: He only breaks the oath if he comes at night or daytime in his statement 'My slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives' because by 'day' is meant 'time', which is general. Fourth objection: An objection arises: If someone swore 'My slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives', its literal meaning is that by 'day' is meant only daytime, and its figurative meaning is that it is used for night, yet you have combined the literal and figurative, and you said that whenever he comes, whether day or night, the slave becomes free. Answer: By 'day' is meant only time through the method of generality of metaphor, which includes both day and night. Some people say that the word 'yawm' is shared between daytime and absolute time, and here absolute time is intended. For this a rule has been stated: If both actions are extended, then 'yawm' means daytime; if both are non-extended, then it means time; if one is extended and the other not, then the verb of the agent is considered, not the verb of the object.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a legal dispute (usul dispute) concerning the interpretation of the word 'yawm' (day) in conditional oaths. The author presents a fourth objection against the Hanafi position that an oath using 'yawm' is broken whether the arrival occurs at day or night. The objector argues that combining the literal meaning (daytime) and figurative meaning (night) is impermissible. The Hanafi response explains that 'yawm' here is used metaphorically to mean 'time' in general, which is a common usage (isti'mal 'amm). This avoids combining literal and figurative meanings because the metaphorical meaning is the intended one. The passage then gives a detailed rule (dabita) for determining whether 'yawm' means daytime or absolute time depending on the nature of the actions involved: if both actions (e.g., oath and arrival) are temporally extended (mumtadd), 'yawm' means daytime; if both are instantaneous (ghayr mumtadd), it means absolute time; if one is extended and the other not, the verb of the agent (the one swearing) governs the interpretation. This rule is specific to Hanafi usul and reflects their method of reconciling literal and figurative uses in legal language.

Key Terms
یوم (yawm): Absolute time (waqt) inclusive of day and night when used in oaths.
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning used via connection (ittisal) between two things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Absolute time (including day and night)
B) Daytime only (nahaar)
C) The time of arrival
D) It depends on the intention of the speaker

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of 'yawm' in oaths as absolute time via majaz is supported by a rule that considers the nature of the actions involved, avoiding the impermissible combination of haqiqah and majaz.

The Dual Nature of 'Lillahi Alayya Sawmu Rajab': Nadhr and Yamin Combined
Analyzing whether a single statement constitutes both a vow and an oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a single utterance can have both a literal and implied legal effect and evaluate the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the statement 'Lillahi alayya sawmu Rajab'.

Core Idea
One statement can be both a vow by wording and an oath by implication.

The statement 'Lillahi alayya sawmu Rajab' appears to be a simple vow. However, a deeper usul analysis reveals that it can also function as an oath. This lesson examines the Hanafi legal dispute on whether this single phrase generates both a vow (nadhr) and an oath (yamin) simultaneously, and the practical consequences that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Statement and the Dispute
Recognize the phrase 'Lillahi alayya sawmu Rajab' and understand that it is being analyzed as both a vow and an oath. Note the different positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it is both; Abu Yusuf says it is either one depending on intention.
2
Understand the Principle of Form vs. Effect
In Hanafi usul, the wording (sighah) of an utterance determines its literal classification, but the legal effect (muijab) may arise from implication. Here, 'Lillahi alayya' is the wording of a vow, but the effect of making permissible things forbidden qualifies as an oath.
This principle is seen elsewhere, e.g., purchasing a close relative results in ownership (by wording) and emancipation (by implication).
3
Analyze the Analogy with Purchasing a Relative Slave
The author draws an analogy: just as buying a mahram slave results in both ownership (by sale) and emancipation (by prophetic hadith), the statement results in both a vow (by wording) and an oath (by effect). Understand how one act can have two legal consequences.
The hadith: 'Whoever becomes the owner of a close relative becomes free.'
4
Evaluate the Two Answers to the Objection
Two responses are given: 1) The statement is a vow in form and an oath in effect, citing Surah al-Tahrim where making halal haram is an oath. 2) The wording is split: 'Lillah' is the oath phrase, 'alayya sawmu Rajab' is the vow phrase. Both responses support the combined effect.
Surah al-Tahrim (66:1) relates to the Prophet making something permissible forbidden, which is considered a yamin.
5
Apply the Ruling: Determine Consequences
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the speaker utters this phrase and then breaks the fast, he must both make up the fast (qada for the vow) and pay expiation (kaffarah for the oath). Under Abu Yusuf, only one obligation arises based on intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وانما اريد النذرو اليمين فيما اذا قال لله على صوم رجب لأنه نذر بصيغته یمین بموجبه کشراء القريب فانه تملک بصيغته وتحرير بموجبه . پانچواں اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص کہے (الله على صوم رجب) اور اس سے نیست نذ را در یمین دونوں کی کر لے یا صرف یمین کی نیت کرے تو آپ کہتے ہیں کہ اس سے نذرا اور یمین دونوں مراد ہو نگے حالانکہ اس کا حقیقی معنی نذر ہے اور مجازی معنی یمین ہے اس وجہ سے کہتے ہیں کہ اس کے فوت ہونے سے نذر کی قضا لا زم آئے گی اور یمین کا کفارہ بھی لیکن یادر ہے کہ یہ اعتراض صرف امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے مذہب پر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ امام ابو یوسف یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ پہلی صورت میں یہ نذر ہوگی اور دوسری صورت میں یمین ہوگی ۔ جواب ..... جواب اس کا یہ ہے کہ یہ اپنے صیغے کے اعتبار سے نذر ہے اور موجب کے اعتبار سے یمین ہے کیونکہ مباح کو حرام کرنا بھی یمین ہے جیسا کہ مباح کو واجب کرنا ۔ جیسا کو سورہ تحریم میں ہے تو متکلم کیلئے صوم رجب کا چھوڑ نا حلال تھا مگر اس نے اسے اپنے اوپر حرام کر دیا یوں یہ یمین ہو گا۔ دوسرا جواب ... بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ اللہ یہ یمین کا صیغہ ہے اور ( علی صوم رجب ( نذر کا صیغہ ہے بہر حال یہ ایسا ہو گا جیسا کہ اپنے قریبی رشتہ دار غلام کو خریدنا اسلئے کہ یہ صیغے کے اعتبار سے تو تملیک یعنی مالک بننا ہے اور موجب کے اعتبار سے اعتاق ہے یعنی وہ غلام آزاد ہو جا یگا اسلئے کہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ جو اپنے قریبی رشتہ دار کا مالک بن جائے وہ آزاد ہے من ملك ذارحم محرم منه عتق عليه (الحديث)
He said: And what is intended is the vow and the oath when he says 'For Allah upon me is fasting Rajab' because it is a vow by its wording and an oath by its implication, like the purchase of a relative, for it is ownership by its wording and emancipation by its implication. Fifth objection: An objection arises that if a person says 'For Allah upon me is fasting Rajab' and intends thereby both the vow and the oath, or intends only the oath, you say that both vow and oath are intended from it, although its literal meaning is vow and its figurative meaning is oath. For that reason they say that upon its omission, the vow must be made up and the expiation for the oath also, but remember that this objection applies only to the school of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad because Imam Abu Yusuf says that in the first case it is a vow and in the second case it is an oath. Answer: The answer to this is that it is a vow by its wording and an oath by its implication, because making permissible things forbidden is also an oath just as making permissible things obligatory. As in Surah al-Tahrim, the speaker had made fasting Rajab permissible but then made it forbidden upon himself, thus it becomes an oath. Second answer: Some people say that 'Allah' is the wording of oath and 'upon me is fasting Rajab' is the wording of vow. In any case, it will be like purchasing a close relative slave, for that is ownership by its wording and emancipation by its implication, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever becomes the owner of a close relative (mahram) becomes free.' (Hadith)
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a legal dispute (usul dispute) concerning the phrase 'Lillahi alayya sawmu Rajab' (For Allah upon me is fasting Rajab). The author begins by stating that this phrase is both a vow (nadhr) by its wording and an oath (yamin) by its implication, analogous to purchasing a close relative slave, which is a sale by wording but results in emancipation by implication. A fifth objection is raised: if someone says this phrase intending both or only an oath, the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that both a vow and an oath are effected, requiring both make-up (qada) for the vow and expiation (kaffarah) for the oath. This is because the literal meaning is a vow, but the figurative meaning is an oath. The objection notes that Imam Abu Yusuf differs, saying that if the intention is both, it is a vow; if only an oath, it is an oath. Two answers are given: first, the statement is a vow by its wording and an oath by its effect, since making permissible things forbidden falls under oath (as in Surah al-Tahrim). Second, some analyze the wording itself: 'Lillah' is the wording of oath, 'alayya sawmu Rajab' is the wording of vow. The analogy with purchasing a relative slave is drawn to show that one statement can have two legal effects: ownership by wording, emancipation by implication. This reflects Hanafi usul's recognition of composite acts where form and effect differ.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, made by saying 'Lillahi alayya...'.
یمین (yamin): An oath that makes something permissible become forbidden, requiring kaffarah if violated.
صیغہ (sighah): The wording or form of an utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is only a vow.
B) It is only an oath.
C) It is both a vow and an oath.
D) It is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A single utterance can yield multiple legal effects when analyzed through wording and implication, as exemplified by 'Lillahi alayya sawmu Rajab' being both a vow and an oath.

Nadhr and Yamin: Combining Vow and Oath in One Phrase
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining nadhr and yamin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between wording (sigha) and effect (mu'aththir) to determine the legal consequences of a phrase that can be both nadhr and yamin.

Core Idea
A single phrase can be both nadhr and yamin: wording determines nadhr, effect determines yamin.

When a person utters a phrase like 'By Allah, I must fast Rajab,' classical Hanafi scholars debated whether it is a nadhr (vow) or a yamin (oath) or both. This lesson examines the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Muhammad, and Imam Abu Yusuf, focusing on how the wording and the effect of the phrase interact. You will learn to separate the linguistic form from the legal consequence, using analogies from the Quran and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Phrase and Its Components
Break the phrase 'by Allah, upon me is fasting Rajab' into two parts: the oath formula (by Allah) and the vow formula (upon me is fasting Rajab). Note that the phrase literally means a nadhr but also implies an oath.
The source discusses a specific phrase but the principles apply generally.
2
State the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf distinguishes between two forms: the first part is nadhr, the second is yamin. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold that the phrase is both simultaneously, requiring both qada and kaffarah if broken.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Analyze the First Response: Nadhr in Wording, Yamin in Effect
The response argues that the wording (sigha) is nadhr, but the effect (making something obligatory) is like a yamin. Support comes from the Quranic precedent of making permissible things haram (Surah al-Tahrim), which is considered a yamin.
This uses qiyas jaliy: analogy from making halal haram to making mubah wajib.
4
Analyze the Second Response: Analogy with Buying a Relative
The second response likens the phrase to buying a close relative slave: the wording implies purchase (tamlik), but the effect is emancipation (itaq) due to the Prophetic hadith. Similarly, the phrase is nadhr in wording but yamin in effect.
This is another qiyas: the hadith 'Man malaka dha rahim muhrim minhu fa huwa hurr' is the basis.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, breaking the phrase requires both qada of the nadhr and kaffarah of the yamin. Under Abu Yusuf, only one obligation applies depending on the specific form (first case = nadhr, second case = yamin).
The tarjih is not given; the text presents both views and responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سے نذر اور یمین دونوں مراد ہوں گے حالانکہ اس کا حقیقی معنی نذر ہے اور مجازی معنی یمین ہے اس وجہ سے کہتے ہیں کہ اس کے فوت ہونے سے نذر کی قضا لازم آئے گی اور یمین کا کفارہ بھی لیکن یاد رہے کہ یہ اعتراض صرف امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے مذہب پر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ امام ابو یوسف یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ پہلی صورت میں یہ نذر ہوگی اور دوسری صورت میں یمین ہوگی۔ جواب ... جواب اس کا یہ ہے کہ یہ اپنے صیغے کے اعتبار سے نذر ہے اور موجب کے اعتبار سے یمین ہے کیونکہ مباح کو حرام کرنا بھی یمین ہے جیسا کہ مباح کو واجب کرنا۔ جیسا کہ سورہ تحریم میں ہے تو متکلم کے لیے صوم رجب کا چھوڑنا حلال تھا مگر اس نے اسے اپنے اوپر حرام کر دیا یوں یہ یمین ہو گا۔ دوسرا جواب ... بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ اللہ یہ یمین کا صیغہ ہے اور (علی صوم رجب) نذر کا صیغہ ہے بہر حال یہ ایسا ہو گا جیسا کہ اپنے قریبی رشتہ دار غلام کو خریدنا اس لیے کہ یہ صیغے کے اعتبار سے تو تملیک یعنی مالک بننا ہے اور موجب کے اعتبار سے اعتاق ہے یعنی وہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا اس لیے کہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ جو اپنے قریبی رشتہ دار کا مالک بن جائے وہ آزاد ہے (من ملك ذا رحم محرم منه عتق عليه) (الحديث)
From ... both nadhr and yamin are intended, although its real meaning is nadhr and metaphorical meaning is yamin. For this reason they say that upon its failure, qada of nadhr and kaffarah of yamin become necessary. But it is remembered that this objection only applies to the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, because Imam Abu Yusuf says that in the first case it is nadhr and in the second case it is yamin. Answer... The answer is that it is nadhr in terms of its wording and yamin in terms of its effect, because making permissible things haram is also yamin just as making permissible things wajib. As in Surah al-Tahrim, the speaker had the option to leave fasting Rajab but he made it haram upon himself, thus it becomes yamin. Second answer... Some people say that 'by Allah' is the wording of yamin and 'upon me is fasting Rajab' is the wording of nadhr. In any case, it will be like buying a close relative slave because it is ownership (tamlik) in terms of wording and emancipation (itaq) in terms of effect, meaning the slave becomes free, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever owns a close relative who is mahram to him, he is free.' (Hadith)

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a person utters a phrase that can be interpreted as both a nadhr (vow) and a yamin (oath). The author explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, such a phrase carries both implications: failure to fulfill it requires both qada of the nadhr and kaffarah of the yamin. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the first formulation is solely nadhr and the second solely yamin. The first response argues that the wording itself indicates nadhr, but the effect (making something obligatory) is like a yamin, analogous to making halal things haram as mentioned in the Quran (Surah al-Tahrim). The second response likens the situation to buying a close relative: the wording implies purchase (tamlik), but the effect is emancipation (itaq) due to the hadith. This illustrates how legal rulings can depend on both apparent wording and underlying effect.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship like fasting, which becomes obligatory upon the person.
یمین (yamin): An oath by Allah or one of His names, often involving making something permissible forbidden or obligatory, requiring kaffarah if broken.
صیغہ (sigha): The wording or form of an expression.
موجب (mu'aththir): The effect or implication of an expression, which may differ from its literal wording.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a phrase can carry both nadhr and yamin obligations when the wording implies one and the effect implies the other, analogized from making halal haram and buying a relative slave.

Dispute on Preference of Haqiqah over Majaz: Imam Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Explaining the disagreement on whether haqiqah or majaz is primary when both are known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the preference of haqiqah over majaz, identify the underlying principle (khalifah in takallum vs. hukm), and apply the dispute to the thamarah al-khilaf in the case of 'This is my son' to an older slave.

Core Idea
When both literal and metaphorical meanings are known, Abu Hanifa prioritizes the literal; the Sahibayn prioritize the metaphorical.

In Hanafi usul, a fundamental disagreement exists between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the priority of literal (haqiqah) versus metaphorical (majaz) meanings when both are valid. This dispute is not merely linguistic but has concrete legal consequences, as seen in oath cases and declarations of sonship. Understanding the underlying principle—whether majaz is a substitute in speech or in ruling—is key to mastering Hanafi interpretive methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disagreement
Determine the exact point of ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn concerning the preference of haqiqah versus majaz when both are known and possible.
Imam Abu Hanifa holds haqiqah awla (primary); the Sahibayn hold majaz awla.
2
Examine the oath examples
Analyze the two examples provided: swearing not to eat 'this wheat' and not to drink 'this Euphrates.' See how each position yields a different ruling on whether the oath is broken.
Abu Hanifa restricts to the literal grain and direct drinking; Sahibayn include bread and vessel-drinking.
3
Grasp the underlying principle
Understand that for Abu Hanifa, majaz is a khalifah (substitute) in speech (takallum); for the Sahibayn, it is a khalifah in ruling (hukm). This determines the conditions for resorting to majaz.
Takallum relates to the word itself; hukm relates to the legal effect.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the dispute to the case of a man saying to an older slave 'This is my son.' Derive the outcome according to each position and note why they differ.
Abu Hanifa takes the metaphorical meaning (emancipation) to avoid meaningless speech; Sahibayn reject it because the ruling of sonship is impossible.
5
Address the objection
Consider the objection: 'Zaid is a lion' should be meaningless under Abu Hanifa's view. Learn why it is not majaz but a literal simile (tashbih) with omitted particle.
The response clarifies that it is a deletion of the letter of similitude, not a metaphorical transfer.

جب حقیقت اور مجاز متعارف ہو :
اور اگر حقیقت مستعمل ہو لیکن مجاز متعارف ہو تو امام صاحب رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک حقیقت اوئی ہوگی اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مجاز اولی ہو گا جیسے (۱) اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی لا اكل من هذه الحنطة تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس سے نفس گندم مراد ہوگی کیونکہ ابال کر اور بھون کر کھا کی جاتی ہے لیکن صاحبین کے نزدیک عموم مجاز کے طور پر گندم کا باطن مراد ہو گا لہذا روٹی کھانے سے بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔

امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے دلائل کا ماحصل :

قوله : وقد تتعذر الحقيقة والمجاز معاً اذا كان الحكم ممتنعاً كما في قوله لامرأته هذه بنتي وهي معروفة النسب
When the literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) are both known: And if the literal is used but the metaphorical is also known, then in the view of Imam Sahib (Abu Hanifa) the literal is primary, and in the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the metaphorical is primary. For example, (1) If someone takes an oath, 'I will not eat from this wheat (hinta),' then according to Imam Sahib, the actual wheat grain is meant, because it is eaten by boiling and roasting. But according to the Sahibayn, by way of common metaphor, the inner part (substance) of the wheat is meant, so he would be guilty of breaking the oath by eating bread. (2) Similarly, if someone takes an oath, 'I will not drink from this Euphrates (al-Furat),' then according to Imam Sahib, he would be guilty only if he drinks by putting his mouth to it, but not by drinking from a cup or vessel. He said: This is built on another principle: that the substitution (khalifah) in speech (takallum) according to him, and according to them it is in the ruling (hukm). And the disagreement appears in his statement to his slave who is older than him: 'This is my son.' This is built. The nature of the disagreement: This disagreement is based on another disagreement: that according to Imam Sahib, majaz is a substitute of haqiqah in speech, and according to the Sahibayn, majaz is a substitute of haqiqah in ruling. For example, when he says to his slave, 'This is my son,' meaning emancipation, then that is a substitute. 'This is my son' [said to a slave] by which sonship is meant. According to Imam Sahib, for majaz to be valid, it is necessary that the literal meaning is correct in terms of the Arabic construction. So if the literal meaning cannot be taken, then the metaphorical meaning is taken. And according to the Sahibayn, since majaz is a substitute of haqiqah in ruling, therefore if the ruling is correct but cannot be acted upon due to some impediment, then recourse is made to the majaz. Summary of the proofs of Imam Sahib and the Sahibayn: Imam Sahib says that when majaz is a substitute for haqiqah in the system (nazm), then speaking with the literal is primary, because the word was coined for the literal meaning. And according to the Sahibayn, since majaz is a substitute for haqiqah in the ruling, therefore the ruling has preference, either due to prevalent usage or because it is general (shamil) covering both haqiqah and majaz. Manifestation of the result of the disagreement: The result of this disagreement appears in the case when a person says to his slave who is older than him, 'This is my son.' According to Imam Sahib, since this is correct in terms of Arabic construction and its meaning is also correct, but due to external evidence it is difficult to take the literal meaning, therefore the metaphorical meaning is taken so that his speech is not meaningless. But it should be clear that according to Imam Sahib, for taking the metaphorical meaning, it is not sufficient that the speech is correct in terms of Arabic construction alone; it is also necessary that its meaning is correct in wording and reason. So if someone says, 'My slave is older than me,' then this speech would be meaningless. And according to the Sahibayn, since for majaz to be a substitute it is necessary that the ruling of haqiqah is valid, and here the ruling is not valid because an older person cannot be a son of a younger one. Objection: The objection is that 'Zaid is a lion' should also be meaningless, because here too the literal meaning cannot be intended. Answer: This is not majaz; rather it is the deletion of the letter of similitude (tashbih) and is haqiqah, i.e., Zaid is like a lion. ...... He said: And it may happen that both haqiqah and majaz are impossible when the ruling is impossible, as in his saying to his wife, 'This is my daughter' while her lineage is known.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), concerning the preference between the literal meaning (haqiqah) and the metaphorical meaning (majaz) when both are known and applicable. The author explains that when a word has both a literal and a well-known metaphorical sense, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning is primary (haqiqah awla), while the Sahibayn hold that the metaphorical meaning is primary (majaz awla). Two examples are given from oaths: (1) swearing not to eat 'this wheat'—Abu Hanifa says the oath is only broken by eating the grain itself, while the Sahibayn include bread because 'wheat' metaphorically refers to its product; (2) swearing not to drink 'this Euphrates'—Abu Hanifa restricts the oath to direct mouth-to-river drinking, while the Sahibayn include drinking with a vessel because the metaphor extends to the water drawn. The underlying principle is that for Abu Hanifa, majaz is a substitute (khalifah) for haqiqah in speech (takallum), meaning that the literal use of the word should prevail since the word was originally coined for that. For the Sahibayn, majaz is a substitute in the ruling (hukm), so the effective legal outcome is given priority. This leads to different conditions: Abu Hanifa requires that the literal meaning be possible in the Arabic construction; if it is impossible, then majaz is used. The Sahibayn require that the ruling of the haqiqah be valid; if it is valid but cannot be implemented due to an external impediment, then majaz is used. The practical fruit (thamrah) of this dispute appears in the case of a man saying to an older slave, 'This is my son.' Abu Hanifa considers the literal sonship impossible due to age, so he takes the majaz meaning of emancipation. The Sahibayn, however, note that the ruling of sonship is impossible (an older cannot be a son of a younger), so they do not take majaz and consider the statement meaningless. An objection is raised about the phrase 'Zaid is a lion'—it is argued that it should be meaningless under Abu Hanifa's view because the literal meaning is impossible. The response clarifies that this is not majaz but rather a case of omitted simile (tashbih), i.e., 'Zaid is like a lion,' which is a literal comparison. The passage then begins a new subtopic: when both haqiqah and majaz are impossible, as in saying to one's wife 'This is my daughter' when her lineage is known—here the statement is simply false. The author's commentary follows the original matn's order, systematically presenting the positions, examples, underlying principles, practical consequences, and a counter-objection with its refutation.

Key Terms
حقيقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning; the meaning for which a word was originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning; a meaning derived from the literal due to a connection (qarinah) with a transferred usage.
خليفة (khalifah): Substitute or successor; majaz is said to be a khalifah of haqiqah either in speech (takallum) or in ruling (hukm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what is the condition for resorting to majaz when both haqiqah and majaz are known?
A) The literal meaning is impossible.
B) The ruling of the literal meaning is valid but cannot be implemented due to an impediment.
C) The speaker intends the metaphorical meaning.
D) The metaphorical meaning is more commonly used than the literal.

Key Takeaway
The priority between haqiqah and majaz depends on whether majaz is considered a substitute in speech (Abu Hanifa) or in ruling (Sahibayn), leading to different legal outcomes in cases like oaths and declarations.

Dispute on Haqiqah and Majaz Between Imam and Sahibayn
Summarizing the proofs and consequence of the haqiqah-majaz dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul dispute on haqiqah vs majaz to derive the practical ruling in a case where both literal and metaphorical meanings are possible.

Core Idea
Imam prefers haqiqah; Sahibayn prefer the ruling of majaz, affecting when metaphor is valid.

This passage examines a foundational ikhtilaf in Hanafi usul: when a word can be taken in its literal (haqiqah) or metaphorical (majaz) sense, which takes priority? Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the literal meaning should be preferred because words are coined for it, while his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), hold that since majaz is a substitute for haqiqah in legal effect, the ruling conveyed by the metaphor takes precedence. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly interpreting ambiguous texts and avoiding incorrect derivations of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
Read the first part of the passage: Imam says haqiqah is preferred because words are coined for real meanings; Sahibayn say the ruling (hukm) of majaz takes precedence due to predominant usage or generality.
The basis of the dispute is whether majaz is a deputy in nazm (order) or hukm (ruling).
2
Understand the Khalifah Mechanism
Imam defines khalifah as substitution in nazm, so the original (haqiqah) is primary. Sahibayn define it as substitution in hukm, so the ruling conveyed by majaz becomes primary.
This conceptual difference drives the different preferences.
3
Apply to the Example of 'This is my son'
Consider a person saying 'هذا ابني' to an older slave. According to Imam, take the metaphorical meaning (i.e., treating him as a son) to avoid pointless speech, but only if the meaning is both verbally and rationally sound. According to Sahibayn, the ruling of haqiqah must be sound; since an older person cannot be a biological son, the speech is pointless.
Notice the additional condition (rational soundness) that Imam requires.
4
Assess the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Imam allows the metaphorical interpretation under certain conditions; Sahibayn do not because the ruling of haqiqah fails. This shows how theoretical preference affects fiqh application.
Both agree that 'Zaid is a lion' is not majaz but haqiqah by ellipsis.
5
Address the Objection About 'Zaid is a Lion'
The objection that 'Zaid is a lion' would also be pointless is answered: it is not majaz but haqiqah through omission of the simile particle, i.e., 'Zaid is like a lion'.
This clarifies that not all non-literal expressions are majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے دلائل کا ماحصل :
امام صاحب کہتے ہیں کہ جب مجاز حقیقت کا نظم میں خلیفہ ہے تو تکلم بالحقیقت اولی ہوگا کیونکہ لفظ کو معنی حقیقی کیلئے وضع کیا گیا ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک چونکہ مجاز حقیقت کا حکم میں خلیفہ ہے لہذا حکم کو ترجیح حاصل ہے یا غالب استعمال کی وجہ سے یا اس سے کہ یہ عام ہے حقیقت کو بھی شامل ہے اور مجاز کو بھی۔ ثمرہ اختلاف کا ظہور :
اس اختلاف کا نتیجہ اس صورت میں ظاہر ہوگا کہ جب کوئی شخص اپنے سے بڑی عمر والے غلام کو کہے هذا ابنی تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک چونکہ یہ عربی ترکیب کے اعتبار سے بھی صحیح ہے اور اس کا مفہوم بھی صحیح ہے مگر خارجی قرائن کے اعتبار سے حقیقی معنی مراد لینا مشکل ہے لہذا مجازی معنی مراد لیا جائے گا تا کہ اس کا کلام لغو نہ ہو مگر یہ بات واضح رہے کہ امام صاحب کے نزد یک مجازی معنی مراد لینے کے لئے صرف عربی ترکیب کے اعتبار سے کلام کا صحیح ہونا کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ بھی ضروری ہے کہ اس کا مفہوم لفتا بھی صیح ہو اور عقل بھی صحیح ہولہذا اگر کسی نے یوں کہا عبدى الاكبر مني حد تو یہ کلام لغو ہو جائے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک چونکہ مجاز کے خلیفہ ہو انے کیلئے حقیقت کے حکم کا صحیح ہونا ضروری ہے اور یہاں حکم تو صحیح نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ بڑا چھوٹے کا بیٹا نہیں بن سکتا۔
اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ زید اسد بھی افو ہونا چاہیے کیونکہ یہاں بھی حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں لیا جا سکتا ہے۔
جواب یہ مجاز نہیں بلکہ حرف تشبیہ کو حذف کرنے کیسا تھ حقیقت ہے یعنی زید کالاسد ہے۔
Summary of the proofs of Imam Sahib and the Sahibayn: Imam Sahib says that when majaz is the deputy (khalifa) of haqiqah in the order [of usage], then speaking with haqiqah is more appropriate because the word is coined for the real meaning. And according to the Sahibayn, since majaz is the deputy of haqiqah in the ruling, therefore the ruling is given precedence, either due to predominant usage or because it is general, encompassing both haqiqah and majaz. The manifestation of the consequence of the difference: The result of this difference will become apparent in the case when a person says to a slave who is older than him: 'هذا ابني' (this is my son). According to Imam Sahib, since this is correct in terms of Arabic composition and its meaning is also correct, but due to external evidence (qara'in) it is difficult to take the real meaning, therefore the metaphorical meaning will be taken so that his speech is not pointless. But it should be clear that according to Imam Sahib, for taking the metaphorical meaning, it is not sufficient that the speech is correct only in terms of Arabic composition; rather, it is also necessary that its meaning is sound verbally and also sound in reason. Therefore, if someone says 'عبدى الاكبر مني حد' (my slave is greater than me in something), this speech will become pointless. And according to the Sahibayn, for majaz to be a deputy, it is necessary that the ruling of haqiqah is sound, and here the ruling is not sound because the older cannot be the son of the younger. Objection: It is objected that 'Zaid is a lion' (زید اسد) should also be pointless because here too the real meaning cannot be taken. Answer: This is not majaz but rather it is haqiqah by way of omitting the particle of similarity, i.e., 'Zaid is like a lion'.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on the priority of haqiqah (literal meaning) vs. majaz (metaphorical meaning) when a word can bear both. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that since majaz is a substitute for haqiqah in the order of speech, the literal meaning should be preferred because words are coined for their real meanings. The Sahibayn counter that majaz is a substitute in terms of the ruling (hukm), so the ruling takes precedence, either due to common usage or because majaz is more general including both. The consequence is shown in an example: if a person says to an older slave 'this is my son', Imam says majaz is used to avoid pointless speech, but only if the meaning is also verbally and rationally sound. If not (e.g., absurd statement), it's pointless. The Sahibayn require the ruling to be sound, which it isn't here. The objection about 'Zaid is a lion' is answered: it's not majaz but an ellipsis of the simile particle, so it remains haqiqah.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): The literal, real meaning for which a word was originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning that is a substitute (khalifah) for haqiqah in some respect.
خلیفہ (khalifah): Deputy or substitute; majaz is a khalifah of haqiqah either in order (nazm) or ruling (hukm).
ثمرہ اختلاف (thamar al-ikhtilaf): The practical consequence of the theoretical difference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a person saying to an older slave 'this is my son', according to Imam Abu Hanifa, what condition must be met to take the metaphorical meaning?
A) The speech must be grammatically correct only.
B) The meaning must be sound both verbally and rationally.
C) The ruling of haqiqah must be sound.
D) The slave must confirm the statement.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dispute on haqiqah vs majaz centers on whether the deputy relationship is in wording (nazm) or ruling (hukm), affecting when metaphorical interpretation is valid.

When Haqiqah and Majaz Are Both Impossible
Analyzes a case where haqiqah and majaz become impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when both haqiqah and majaz are impossible in a legal utterance and apply the Hanafi principle that such speech becomes لغو (nonsense), with its legal consequences.

Core Idea
When neither literal nor figurative meaning can apply, the utterance is void and carries no legal effect.

This lesson examines a classic usul scenario: a husband calls his wife 'my daughter.' In certain conditions, both the literal meaning (she is his daughter) and the figurative meaning (divorce) are impossible. The Hanafi school holds that such speech becomes meaningless, and the only recourse is judicial separation if the husband insists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Possible Meanings
The husband's statement 'this is my daughter' can be taken literally (haqiqah: she is his biological daughter) or figuratively (majaz: he intends divorce). Recognize both.
2
Check Conditions for Literal Impossibility
Literal meaning is impossible if her lineage is known (she cannot be his biological daughter) or if her age makes it impossible (she is too old or too young to be his daughter).
3
Check Conditions for Figurative Impossibility
Figurative meaning (divorce) is impossible because declaring her as his daughter implies their marriage is invalid (since marriage to a daughter is prohibited), so divorce cannot be valid.
4
Conclude on the Statement
When both meanings are impossible, the statement is considered لغو (nonsense) and has no legal effect—neither lineage nor divorce.
5
Apply the Exception and Dispute
If she is of unknown lineage and younger than him, lineage is established. However, some scholars require the wife's confirmation before lineage is finalized, allowing the husband to retract before that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتو لد لمثله أو أكبر سنا منه حتى لا تقع الحرمة بذلك ابداً. اور کبھی حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں محمد رہو جاتے ہیں اور دونوں کا حکم ممکن العمل نہیں رہتا اس صورت میں کلام لغو ہو جاتا ہے۔ مثلا کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا ھذه بنتی حالانکہ اس کا نسب مشہور ہے لیکن اس کی عمر اتنی چھوٹی ہے کہ اگر یہ شوہر بالغ ہونے کے فورا بعد نکاح کر لیتا تو اس کی بھی اس جیسی لڑکی پیدا ہو سکتی تھی ۔ یا اس کی بیوی عمر میں اس سے بڑی ہے تو یہاں حقیقی معنی بھی حعذر ہے اس لئے کہ ان دونوں صورتوں میں اس عورت کا اسکی بیٹی ہونا مشکل ہے۔ اور مجازی معنی بھی متعذر ہے یعنی انست طلق اس لئے کہ طلاق پہلے نکاح کے صحیح ہونے کا تقاضا کرتی ہے اور بیٹی ہونا تقاضا کرتا ہے کہ نکاح ہوا ہی نہیں ہے کیونکہ بیٹی سے نکاح کسی بھی شریعت کی رو سے جائز نہیں ۔ جب نکاح نہیں ہوا ہے تو طلاق بھی صحیح نہیں ہے ہاں اگر یہ شوہر ھذہ بنتی کہنے پر اصرار کرے تو قاضی ان کے درمیان جدائی کردے گا کیونکہ اصرار کرنے کی وجہ سے وہ عورت کے حق جماع کے بارے میں ظالم ہو گیا لہذا تفریق ضروری ہے۔ جیسا کہ محبوب اور حنین کے بارے میں ہے۔ اور اگر دونوں شرطیں نہ پائی جائیں یعنی وہ مجہولتہ النسب ہو اور عمر میں اس سے چھوٹی بھی ہو تو نسب ثابت ہو جائیگا اور حرمت واقع ہو جائیگی۔ بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ اگر عمر میں چھوٹی ہو اور مجہولۃ النسب ہو تو بھی یہی حکم ہے اس لئے کہ نسب اس وقت تک ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا جب تک مقرلہ تصدیق نہ کرے اور اس کے تصدیق سے پہلے اس کا رجوع کرنا بھی صحیح ہے۔
And if he can father a child like her or older, then the prohibition never occurs. And sometimes both haqiqah and majaz are prevented, and both cannot be acted upon; in that case the speech becomes nonsense. For example, a man said to his wife 'this is my daughter' while her lineage is known, but her age is so small that if the husband had married immediately after puberty, he could have fathered a girl like her; or his wife is older in age. In these cases the literal meaning is impossible because in both cases it is difficult for the woman to be his daughter. And the figurative meaning is also impossible, i.e., that he intended divorce, because divorce requires the validity of the marriage, and being a daughter requires that marriage never occurred, since marriage with a daughter is not permitted in any law. When there is no marriage, divorce is not valid. However, if the husband insists on saying 'this is my daughter', the qadi will separate them because by insisting he becomes oppressive regarding the wife's right to intercourse, so separation is necessary. This is like the case of Mahbub and Hunayn. And if both conditions are not met, i.e., she is of unknown lineage and younger than him, then lineage is established and prohibition occurs. Some people say that even if she is younger and of unknown lineage, the same ruling applies because lineage cannot be established until the woman confirms, and before her confirmation, he may retract.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a specific legal scenario where a husband declares his wife to be his daughter. The author discusses the conflict between haqiqah (literal meaning: she is actually his daughter) and majaz (figurative meaning: he intends divorce). When both are impossible, the statement is considered لغو (nonsense). The ruling is that if the husband insists, the qadi separates them due to harm to the wife's rights. If the wife is younger and of unknown lineage, the lineage is established, making marriage prohibited. The passage also mentions a difference of opinion: some scholars hold that even with these conditions, the ruling requires the wife's confirmation before lineage is finalized.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word as originally set for its referent
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning used due to a connection with the literal meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The statement is effective as a divorce
B) The statement establishes lineage and prohibits marriage
C) Both literal and figurative meanings are impossible, so the statement is void
D) The husband must be punished

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when both literal and figurative meanings of a statement are impossible, the statement is void and has no legal effect; the only remedy is judicial separation if the speaker insists.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Five Qara'in for Preferring Majaz over Haqiqah
Enumerating five indicators for metaphorical interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five qara'in, apply them to furu' examples, and evaluate the Hanafi position in the lahm oath dispute using etymological analysis.

Core Idea
Context, custom, etymology, speaker state, and full speech all override literal meaning.

This passage from the sharh of Nur al-Anwar systematically presents five indicators (qara'in) that lead a jurist to interpret a word metaphorically rather than literally. Understanding these indicators is crucial for accurate istinbat, as many Quranic verses and hadiths require metaphorical interpretation. We will work through each qarina with the examples provided, including the well-known dispute on whether an oath to avoid meat (lahm) includes fish.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first qarina: ‘urf wa ‘adah
Recognize that customary usage (urf) can transfer a word from its literal meaning to a new shar‘i meaning. Example: salat and hajj originally meant supplication and intent, but now refer to specific rituals. If someone vows to pray, they mean the ritual, not mere supplication.
Hanafis give great weight to urf; it can even override the literal meaning in oaths and contracts.
2
Analyze the second qarina: dalalat nafs al-lafz (etymological indication)
Examine how a word's derivation and root meaning can exclude certain items. The word lahm is derived from al-tiham (strength) and implies blood; fish lacks blood, so it is not included in an oath against eating meat.
This step involves both linguistic analysis and custom: fish-sellers are not called butchers.
3
Apply the third qarina: siyaq al-kalam (context)
Consider the surrounding speech and circumstances. Example: 'Divorce your wife if you are a man' – the phrase 'if you are a man' shows it is a threat, not a genuine divorce authorization. Similarly, Qur'an 18:29 ('Let him who wills believe, and let him who wills disbelieve') is a challenge, not permission, because of the subsequent warning of Hellfire.
The preceding or following text often clarifies whether speech is literal or figurative.
4
Evaluate the fourth qarina: state of the speaker (yamin fawri)
Assess the emotional state of the speaker. An immediate oath (yamin fawri) in extreme anger overrides the literal condition. If a husband in anger says 'If you go out, you are divorced', and the anger subsides, later exiting does not trigger divorce because the speaker's state indicated a non-literal intent (threat rather than conditional divorce).
This qarina requires attention to the speaker's psychological context, not just the words.
5
Derive the ruling using the fifth qarina: dalalat tamam al-kalam (indication of the complete speech)
Interpret the hadith 'innama al-a‘malu bi al-niyat' by considering the full import. Literally it would mean no action is valid without intention, but many actions (e.g., habitual acts) occur without intention and are valid. Thus the intended meaning is that reward depends on intention, not worldly validity. This is confirmed by consensus (ijma‘).
The 'complete speech' includes the implications of the entire utterance within its legal context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كقوله عليه السلام إنما الأعمال بالنيات وقوله عليه السلام رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان. عمل بالمجاز کے قرائن
یہاں سے مصنف وہ قرائن بتلاتے ہیں جس کی وجہ سے مجاز پر عمل کیا جاتا ہے اور حقیقت کو چھوڑ دیا جاتا ہے؟
(۱) قرینه اول پہلا قرینہ جس کی وجہ سے حقیقت کو چھوڑ دیا جاتا ہے وہ ہے عرف اور عادت جیسا کہ صلاة اور حج کی نذر لفظ میں صلاة کا معنی دعاء اور حج کا معنی قصد وارادہ ہے لیکن پھر ان کو ارکان معلومہ کی طرف منتقل کر دیا گیا ہے۔ اور پہلے معنی کو چھوڑ دیا گیا لہذا اگر کسی نے صلاة اور حج کی نذر مانی تو اس سے دعاء اور قصد وارادہ مراد نہیں ہونگے بلکہ ارکان معلومہ مراد ہوں گے۔ (۲) قرینہ ثانی دوسرا قرینہ جس کی وجہ سے حقیقت کو چھوڑا جاتا ہے فی نفسه دلالت کی وجہ سے یعنی لفظ اپنے ماخذ اشتقاق اور حروف کے معنی کے اعتبار سے اس بات پر دلالت کرے کہ یہاں لفظ کا حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں ہے مثلا لفظ کو ایسے معنی مشتقی کے لئے وضع کیا گیا ہو جس میں قوت ہے تو اس سے وہ چیز نکل جائیگی جس میں ضعف پایا جاتا ہے جیسے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی والله لا آكل لحما فسمك کو شامل نہیں ہوگا اس لئے کہ لحم التحام سے مشتق ہے اور وہ شدت اور قوت کو کہتے ہیں اور شدت بغیر خون کے نہیں ہو سکتی اور سمک میں خون نہیں ہوتا اس لئے عرف میں سمک کے بائع کو بائع اللحم نہیں کہا جاتا۔ امام مالک کا مذہب مگر امام مالک کہتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں بھی وہ حانث ہو جائیگا اس لئے کہ قرآن میں پر بھی سمک کا اطلاق کیا گیا ہے۔ اور اسی طرح کسی نے قسم اٹھائی (كل مملوك لي فهو حر) تو يه مكاتب كو شامل نہیں ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ اس میں مملوکیت کا معنی ضعف کے ساتھ پایا جاتا ہے کیونکہ وہ من کل الوجوہ مملوک نہیں ہے بلکہ من بعض الوجوہ مملوک ہے۔
قرینہ ثالثہ تیسرا قرینہ سیاق ہے یعنی سیاق الکلام اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہوگا کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں بلکہ مجاز مراد ہے جیسا کہ کوئی شخص کسی کو کہے ( طلق امرأتك إن كنت رجلا ) اس کلام کی حقیقت تو توکیل بالطلاق ہے لیکن (إن كنت رجلا) اس بات پر قرینہ ہے کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں ہے بلکہ مخاطب کو صرف عاجز کرنا اور ڈرانا مراد ہے۔
اس طرح قرآن حکیم میں ہے فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر ( یہاں اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف سے کفر کی اجازت نہیں دی گئی ہے بلکہ یہ تحدید اور تہدید کے لئے ہے کیونکہ آگے قرینہ موجود ہے اور وہ ہے نار جہنم کی وعید ۔
قرینہ رابعہ چوتھا قرینہ کوئی ایسا معنی ہے جس کا تعلق متکلم کے ساتھ ہو کہ وہ دلالت کرتا ہو کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں ہے جیسے کہ یمین فوری یعنی وہ قسم جو انتہائی غصے کی حالت میں اٹھائی جائے تو اگر شوہر نے غصے کی حالت میں بیوی کو کہہ دیا (إن خرجت فأنت طالق) تو اس کلام کی حقیقت تو یہی کہ جب بھی عورت نکلے تو اس کو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی لیکن متکلم کی حالت اس بات پر دلالت کرتی ہے کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں ہے لہذا اگر عورت غصہ ٹھنڈا ہونے کے بعد گھر نکل گئی تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
قرینہ خامسہ پانچواں قرینہ مکمل کلام کی دلالت ہے یعنی جس محل میں کلام واقع ہے وہ اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہو کہ یہاں حقیقت نہیں بلکہ مجاز مراد ہے جیسے حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا فرمان (إنما الأعمال بالنيات) اس کا حقیقی معنی تو یہ ہے کہ کوئی عمل بھی بغیر نیت کے صحیح نہ ہو۔ حالانکہ بہت سے اعمال بغیر نیت کے واقع ہوتے ہیں لہذا یہاں مجازی معنی مراد لیا جائے گا یعنی (ثواب الأعمال بالنیات) یا (حكم الأعمال بالنیات) یعنی اعمال کا آخرت میں ثواب نیت سے ملے گا۔ اور حکم دو قسم کا ہو سکتا ہے: (1) حکم دنیوی (2) حکم اخروی
اب یہاں حکم اخروی یعنی ثواب تو بالاجماع مراد ہے لہذا حکم دنیوی مراد نہیں لیا جائے گا
Like his saying (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions', and his saying (peace be upon him): 'My community is relieved of error and forgetfulness.' Operation with metaphor (majaz) and its indicators. From here the author explains the indicators due to which majaz is acted upon and the literal meaning (haqiqah) is abandoned. (1) First indicator: The first indicator due to which the literal is abandoned is custom and habit, such as in the vow of prayer (salat) and pilgrimage (hajj), the meaning of salat is supplication and hajj is intention and desire, but then they were transferred to the known pillars and the first meaning was abandoned. Therefore, if someone makes a vow of salat or hajj, supplication and intention are not meant but the known pillars are meant. (2) Second indicator: The second indicator due to which the literal is abandoned is because of indication from the word itself, i.e., the word, by its origin of derivation and the meaning of its letters, indicates that the literal meaning is not intended here. For example, if a word is coined for a derived meaning that has strength, then that which has weakness is excluded. As if someone swears 'By Allah, I will not eat meat (lahman)', it will not include fish, because 'lahm' is derived from 'al-tiham' which means intensity and strength, and intensity cannot occur without blood, and fish has no blood. Therefore, in custom, the seller of fish is not called a seller of meat. Imam Malik's school: But Imam Malik says that in this case he still breaks the oath, because in the Qur'an 'lahm' is also applied to fish. And similarly, if someone swears 'Every slave I own is free', it will not include the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) because the meaning of being owned in his case is accompanied by weakness, since he is not owned in all respects but is owned in some respects. (3) Third indicator: The third indicator is context (siyaq), i.e., the context of speech indicates that the literal is not intended but the metaphorical is intended. As when someone says to another 'Divorce your wife if you are a man', the literal meaning of this speech is authorization of divorce, but 'if you are a man' is an indicator that the literal meaning is not intended; rather, it is only to humiliate and threaten the addressee. Similarly in the Holy Qur'an: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' – here Allah did not give permission for disbelief; rather, it is for challenge and threat, because there is an indicator ahead, which is the warning of Hellfire. (4) Fourth indicator: The fourth indicator is a meaning connected to the speaker that indicates that the literal is not intended here, such as an immediate oath (yamin fawri), i.e., an oath taken in a state of extreme anger. If a husband says to his wife in anger 'If you go out, you are divorced', the literal meaning of this speech is that whenever the woman goes out, divorce will occur. But the speaker's state indicates that the literal meaning is not intended. Therefore, if the woman goes out after the anger has cooled, divorce does not occur. (5) Fifth indicator: The fifth indicator is the indication of the complete speech, i.e., the context in which the speech occurs indicates that the literal is not intended but the metaphorical is intended. Like the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions'. The literal meaning of this is that no action is valid without intention, even though many actions occur without intention. Therefore, a metaphorical meaning is taken here: 'Reward of actions is by intentions' or 'The ruling of actions is by intentions', i.e., the reward of actions in the Hereafter comes from intention. And the ruling can be of two types: (1) worldly ruling, (2) otherworldly ruling. Here, the otherworldly ruling, i.e., reward, is intended by consensus, so the worldly ruling is not taken.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Nur al-Anwar explains the five qara'in (indicators) that lead to interpreting a word or phrase metaphorically (majaz) rather than literally (haqiqah). The author begins by citing two hadiths that are themselves examples of majaz: 'Actions are but by intentions' and 'My community is relieved of error and forgetfulness.' He then enumerates the five qara'in with examples. The first qarina is 'urf wa 'adah (custom and habit). For instance, the words salat and hajj originally meant supplication and intent, but in Islamic law they have been transferred to specific rituals. So if someone vows to perform salat or hajj, they mean the rituals, not the linguistic meanings. The second qarina is dalalat nafs al-lafz (indication from the word itself based on etymology). The word lahm (meat) is derived from al-tiham (strength), implying blood; fish lacks blood, so it is not included in an oath to avoid meat. The Hanafi school takes this view, while Imam Malik argues that the Qur'an uses lahm for fish, so it should be included. The third qarina is siyaq al-kalam (context). An example is a husband telling his wife to divorce if he is a man—the context shows it's a threat, not a genuine authorization. Similarly, the Qur'anic verse 'faman sha'a falyu'min wa man sha'a falyakfur' is a challenge, not permission. The fourth qarina is a state of the speaker, such as an oath in extreme anger (yamin fawri). If a husband, in anger, says his wife is divorced if she goes out, but the anger subsides and she goes out, the divorce does not occur because the literal meaning is overridden by the speaker's emotional state. The fifth qarina is dalalat tamam al-kalam (indication of the complete speech). The hadith 'innama al-a'malu bi al-niyat' literally means actions are invalid without intention, but since many actions occur without intention, the intended meaning is that reward depends on intention, as confirmed by consensus. The author distinguishes between worldly and otherworldly rulings, stating that the hadith refers to the latter.

Key Terms
قرینہ (qarina): An indicator or contextual clue that leads to preferring the metaphorical meaning over the literal.
حقیقت (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as coined for its original usage.
مجاز (majaz): The metaphorical or transferred meaning of a word, used when a qarina prevents the literal meaning.
عرف (urf): Customary usage that determines meaning, as in the transfer of salat from supplication to ritual prayer.
سیاق (siyaq): The context of speech, including preceding and following phrases, indicating intended meaning.
یمین فوری (yamin fawri): An oath immediately prompted by a strong emotional state, such as anger, that overrides literal interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth qarina (dalalat tamam al-kalam), what is the correct interpretation of the hadith 'innama al-a'malu bi al-niyat'?
A) No action is valid without intention.
B) Reward of actions depends on intention.
C) Actions must be performed with both intention and ritual purity.
D) The hadith is abrogated and not applicable.

Key Takeaway
The five qara'in (custom, etymology, context, speaker's state, and complete speech) are essential tools for a Hanafi jurist to determine when a text must be interpreted metaphorically.
